From rsteff at comcast.net  Wed Oct  1 11:24:21 2008
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Wed, 01 Oct 2008 11:24:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Resolved: Google Earth in Ubuntu
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0809301907310.6739@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>
References: <48D9A754.40108@comcast.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0809232240350.6762@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>	<48DA5406.8030601@comcast.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0809241802150.6740@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>	<48DAEBBF.7080006@comcast.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0809242218140.6740@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>	<48DC0957.8060901@comcast.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0809252047030.6682@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>	<48DC6D47.3030701@comcast.net>	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0809260816290.6685@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>	<48E25EFC.4020003@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0809301907310.6739@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>
Message-ID: <48E3C055.2050601@comcast.net>

Conclusion:

It appears that the problem was ownership of /home/rsteff/.config/Google 
and /home/rsteff/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf; each showed up as 
root:root. Changing the ownership of /home/rsteff/.config/Google to 
rsteff:rsteff and effectively deleting 
/home/rsteff/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf (I just renamed it, 
after changing its ownership) seem to be what was needed. Google Earth 
now starts and runs from the Applications > Internet menu, and I can fly 
to various addresses.

A shortened story of How I reached that conclusion follows. The short 
short version is that strace lead to a permissions problem which showed 
the ownership problem.


 Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> On Tue, 30 Sep 2008, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
>   
>> Well, just having the GUI does seem like it's not connecting with the GE
>> server. I don't get that login message, but I don't get it on my XP
>> laptop, either. But I do get a message about Google Earth initialization
>> while the image of the earth is spinning in from the Atlantic ocean to
>> the center of North America. And, there's a message about "My Places."
>> Neither of those happen on my Ubuntu machine.
>>     
> The obvious question is: are you running behind a web proxy?  
No. At least, I don't think so. I connect to Comcast through a router. I 
don't recall anything about a proxy.
> A packet
> trace could verify the quality of the connection.
>   
Traceroute? I'd need to know where to have it look. I don't imagine it 
would be at the Google Earth web site, earth.google.com. Trace route 
only reveals my own machine, my router, and one hop in Seattle (9    
te-3-2.car1.Seattle1.Level3.net    4.79.104.105    16.588ms). All the 
rest (there are 31 hops) show "no reply"
> Looks like My Places is a .kml file in ~/.googleearth.  Also, I see a
> lot of these when doing an strace:
>
> stat64("/home/ckonstanski/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf", {st_mo
> de=S_IFREG|0600, st_size=2313, ...}) = 0
>   
I tried strace with only -o as an option. I got a file with 49356 lines, 
so I won't post the whole thing, here. There were a lot of entries 
stating that there was "no such file or directory." In most of them 
(all?), it was sub directories that do not exist. The filename in 
question was eventually found in another sub directory.
> I was not aware that there is a ~/.config/Google directory.  Might want
> to delete that, and ~/.googleearth, during the next reinstall.  strace
> is handy!
>   
----------------------------------------------------------------------
stat64("/home/rsteff/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf", 
{st_mode=S_IFREG|0600, st_size=676, ...}) = 0
open("/home/rsteff/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf", 
O_RDONLY|O_LARGEFILE) = -1 EACCES (Permission denied)
----------------------------------------------------------------------

/home/rsteff/.config/Google and 
/home/rsteff/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf both were owned by 
root. I changed them to rsteff:rsteff and started up GE. I got an error 
message box (but didn't write it down :-| -- something about not being 
able to find a file related to root, and that it would create one) but 
the problem remains.

I'm a little confused about why there is a GoogleEarthPlus.conf because 
1) I'm not running GEPlus, and 2) a little later there is also this:

----------------------------------------------------------------------
stat64("/usr/lib/googleearth/Google.conf", 0xbffb4630) = -1 ENOENT (No 
such file or directory)
----------------------------------------------------------------------

Such a file still does not exist. I renamed the .conf file, on the guess 
that the program would create a new one. It turns out that my guess was 
correct.

Conclusion:

It appears that the problem was ownership of /home/rsteff/.config/Google 
and /home/rsteff/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf. Changing the 
ownership of /home/rsteff/.config/Google and effectively deleting 
/home/rsteff/.config/Google/GoogleEarthPlus.conf seem to be what was needed.

Thanks for the introduction to strace. There remains much I don't know 
about it, but what I do know I can use in the future.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

This information is all broadcast by your browser: when I visit that
site, it lists my ip, browser config, etc...

It's no different than looking into a mirror -- the wall didn't
somehow steal a live video feed of your face :)

The web site should have a privacy policy somewhere that details what
(if anything) they do with that information.  Most places discard it
excepting for the standard log files.  Since this instance is in a
signature on a post, it's probably not even apparent to the person who
posted.

Michael,

If it bothers you, Firefox allows you to change its User Agent 
advertisement. Google for "firefox change user agent". You can do it 
manually via the general.useragent.override option or, more easily, 
install an extension that will do it for you.

Also, choosing the About Firefox menu option (wherever it lives on 
your system) will show you the default user agent settings. In my 
case, it's "Mozilla/5.0 (Macintosh; U; Intel Mac OS X 10.5; en-US; 
rv:1.9.0.3) Gecko/2008092414 Firefox/3.0.3" -- which obviously gives 
everyone a pretty good idea of my workstation platform. :-)

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From gently at gmail.com  Thu Oct  2 10:50:40 2008
From: gently at gmail.com (chris (fool) mccraw)

	<c834ec80810012323p45c9d59et3b7d5f7bd7982233@mail.gmail.com>
	<alpine.OSX.2.00.0810021014430.9258@guild.galois.com>
Message-ID: <ae2db4620810021050v79bbc755y4584d5aa9ed15ac7@mail.gmail.com>

On Thu, Oct 2, 2008 at 10:20, Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com> wrote:

> If it bothers you, Firefox allows you to change its User Agent
> advertisement. Google for "firefox change user agent". You can do it
> manually via the general.useragent.override option or, more easily,
> install an extension that will do it for you.


of course, changing your UA will often lock you out of sites that are
too fancy and demand that your browser be among the approved list, as
i'm sure you've already experienced even with your real firefox user
agent string.  making it something totally fabricated can break many
sites--often, for no good reason, but that's not much consolation when
they don't work.  stealing the UA of another popular browser might
yield slightly better results, but will likely result in a different
group of broken sites as well.  yay for web standards, boo for
browsers and page-writers that don't play nice.

"Fingerprint" is perhaps a fairly appropriate metaphor for what's going 
on here, as a person quite literally leaves their fingerprints whenever 
they use their fingers to touch things (unless they bother to wear 
gloves all the time).

So, for example, a restaurant actually collects fingerprints off of 
patron's glasses constantly....
...but, in general, while restaurants do collect those fingerprints, 
they often destroy them fairly rapidly, by washing the glasses.

If this bothers a person, they may choose to wear gloves (along with 
other measures to avoid leaving personal identifiers, such as bringing 
one's own utensils, shaving all body hair off to reduce tissue shedding, 
drinking through a straw that they keep with them, making sure not to 
use unsecured bathrooms, wearing a mask or altering their face to trip 
up facial-recognition scanners, and speaking through a voice alteration 
device (or not talking)).... or they can simply avoid restaurants which 
are known for collecting and divulging data about their patrons?

-Bop

Moving on to the next issue...

I have a couple of Win98 laptops that used to be able to see the Samba 
shares on my SuSE installation. I've discovered that Ubuntu has a 
slightly different way of sharing. It looks more like sharing on XP than 
the old way. And, from XP, I can see and access the directories I have 
"shared" on my Ubuntu machine. The problem I'm having is getting to 
those directories from my Win98 machines. Win98 sees the Ubuntu machine, 
but when I try to look at the machine, I'm presented with a login 
dialog. On the Ubuntu machine, the only password I've set is for my 
regular user, and that password does not work in the dialog I see from 
Win98. Also, that dialog does not offer the option of specifying a user.

I've done some Googling and found a number of things that don't seem to 
relate. However, there was one that talked about creating a password 
using smbpasswd. What user is samba using when I try to connect from 
Win98? On my SuSE installation samba understood that rsteff was talking 
to rsteff (I have the same user name on all three machines).

On Tue, Sep 30, 2008 at 03:30:00AM -0700, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> ...  I ordered a Trendnet TEW-424UB 54M G card from Newegg 
> ($10 after rebate) which appears to work with the zd1211rw
> driver according to:
>    http://linuxwireless.org/en/users/Devices/USB
> But of course, the manufacturers like to change the chips without
> changing the device model number.   I may be out of luck.

Out of luck.  The currently shipping version of the Trendnet
card is 3.1R, which uses the Realtek 8187B chipset.  Yet again.
I've ordered a couple of other adapters that claim to use the
Zydas chipset, and will let you folks know of the results.

Linux Wifi is partly hindered by drivers, mostly hindered by
being unable to determine which currently shipping products
use which chipsets.  If either or both Wifi dongles work, I
will start a list on the PLUG wiki, which may help other people
with their buying decisions.

Yes, I know I can use ndiswrapper, but that is not well supported
by other linux tools, such as Network Manager.  The idea is not
to just make do, it is to purchase the best, and reward vendors
who support us.

Keith

I'm guessing you mean the sign in the sig by the user andrewweb. It's
like Rogan says. The image is dynamically generaged, and isn't even
being served by reactos.org. In addition to the User-Agent string,
there's also IP/geolocation involved. (Now I know that the Midland
Branch of the Multnomah Country Library uses NTT America).  The image
is being served from danasoft.com. If you go to their home page it
will show you examples of the various graphics they have including one
that says "Your in Portland" (well, I am, at least). It's just a
gimmick.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sun Oct  5 11:46:24 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sun, 5 Oct 2008 11:46:24 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] USB Wifi - My Essentials ME1001-USB ver. 1000 works!
Message-ID: <20081005184624.GB14904@gate.kl-ic.com>


I am typing this on an Ubuntu Gutsy 8.04 laptop, connected through a
MyEssentials ME1001-USB ver.1000  USB Wireless G adapter, purchased
yesterday ( 2008 Oct 4 ) at Fry's for $24.99 .  Red box.

My Essentials is part of Belkin, and it is not surprising that 
lsusb reports:     ID 050d:705c Belkin Components

It uses the Zydas chipset and the zd1211rw driver.  The ZD1211 is
apparently the only wireless USB chipset currently available that
works out of the box.  See the recommendations at:
http://linuxwireless.org/en/users/Devices/USB

This will provide wifi for my sister until the Realtek RTL8187B
driver ( 0bda:8189 ), which she needs for her builtin mini PCI-E
card, moves beyond early alpha status.  Otherwise, the only way
to use the B version is with ndiswrapper, which doesn't play
nice with Network Manager, the preferred Ubuntu wireless manager.   
ndiswrapper may work better with other AP management systems.  

The Trendnet TEW-424UB and the Belkin FD7050 used to have the
Zydas chipset, and worked, but now they also use the RTL8187B
chip and have the same driver problems. 

If I was willing to use ndiswrapper, I would probably get a 500mW
Alfa from local wireless guy Rick Lindahl's Invictus Networks:

https://shop.invictusnetworks.com/detail.php?id=16104

It is a little larger, but ought to have incredible range, especially
with the big antenna and the long USB cable.  

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

I was unclear.  The older Realtek drivers for the RTL8187 (non B)
did work - rt73 and r8187 IIRC.   The B version breaks these
drivers, and the new version is still under development.  The
improved Realtek driver will be a pretty good option in six months
or so - active development.   Noble souls that enjoy helping out
as beta testers might choose this chipset for that very reason.

I have a new TEW-424UB which I just bought from Newegg with the
RTL8187B.  I will give it to somebody who will actively participate,
coding or beta testing, in the new driver development.

On Sun, 5 Oct 2008 11:46:24 -0700
Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> dijo:

> This will provide wifi for my sister until the Realtek RTL8187B
> driver ( 0bda:8189 ), which she needs for her builtin mini PCI-E
> card, moves beyond early alpha status.  Otherwise, the only way
> to use the B version is with ndiswrapper, which doesn't play
> nice with Network Manager, the preferred Ubuntu wireless manager.   
> ndiswrapper may work better with other AP management systems.  

Everyone seems to love Network Manager, but I hate it. I now have
wireless working fine with the internal Intel 4965agn in my T61, but
even so when I click the drop-down box in Network Manager to see
available networks it usually displays nothing. I know the networks are
there because I can find them from the terminal. I don't know what is
wrong with Network Manager, but it has been broken for as long as I've
owned this computer.

I replaced Network Manager with wicd and it is a joy. Works first time,
every time. You have to add a wicd repository for it to show up in
Synaptic, or just download the .deb if you can find it. There are
installation instructions in a thread on the Ubuntu forums.

The only problem with wicd is that it has a hard-coded timeout and it
is too short. And if it can't connect to the network you selected
within the timeout period it moves on to the next network without even
asking you. Thus, I have to watch what I've actually connected to. At
PSU there are multiple networks, but I won't get a DHCP lease if I
connect to one of the faculty or staff networks that I am not
authorized to use. I still love it, though, because it is so simple and
it works. "Works" is the big plus here.

Bear in mind, high transmit power does you no good unless the thing
you are talking to has a similarly high transmit power.  All the extra
tx power does is evaporate more moisture from surrounding trees and
interfere with your neighbors trying to operate on the same (or
nearby) channel.

Does anyone know of an ISP that supports bonding multiple DSL 
connections to achieve higher speeds.  In particular I'd like to get 
higher upload speeds.  This could be through multilink PPP or other 
methods.  I suppose that with a colo, virtual machine, or even a shell 
account one could create multiple PPP tunnels and bond them on the 
remote end.  Another alternative might be multihoming, but my 
understanding of this is that while aggregate speeds would improve, it 
would not improve the speed of an individual connection such as an ssh 
session.  Thoughts?

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Oct  6 12:35:01 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 6 Oct 2008 12:35:01 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Adding USB IDs to Wireless USB driver
Message-ID: <20081006193501.GA3343@gate.kl-ic.com>


I just got wireless USB working on my SL5 laptop.  There was a lot to
figure out, so I am sharing it here.

I have a MyEssentials ME-1001-USB wireless adapter, which contains a
Zydas zd1211b wireless chipset ( bought from Fry's on saturday October
4 2008.  Note that chipsets change without warning!).  The device is
plug and play on an Ubuntu Heron 8.04 system (with kernel 2.6.24) but
not so with Scientific Linux 5 kernel 2.6.18-8.1.6.el5 . 

The SL5  zd1211rw.ko  driver is missing most of recent the USB device IDs.
So, it needs recompiling with some additions as shown here:

 http://linuxwireless.org/en/users/Drivers/zd1211rw/AddID

What follows is the recipe that works for Red Hat style RPM systems, and
something similar will probably work for SUSE.    I will let you Ubuntu
Debian Slackware Gentoo folks figure it out on your own.  CentOS documentation
refers to /usr/src/rpmbuild instead of /usr/src/redhat , but the latter is
where the files show up.

I found the source files for my kernel here:

 ftp://fr.rpmfind.net/linux/sourceforge/l/li/linux-ntfs/kernel-2.6.18-8.1.6.el5.src.rpm

Then the standard redhat style kernel preparation, as root:

 rpm -Uvh kernel-2.6.18-8.1.6.el5.src.rpm
 cd /usr/src/redhat/SPECS/
 rpmbuild -bp --target=i686 kernel-2.6.spec
 cd /usr/src/redhat/BUILD/kernel-2.6.18
 chmod a+w -R *            # this allows you to work as a normal user

# do these as a normal user

 cd /usr/src/redhat/BUILD/kernel-2.6.18/linux-2.6.18.i686/drivers/net/wireless/zd1211rw/
 cp -a zd_usb.c zd_usb.c.orig

# option one ----------------------------------------------------------------
# download   http://www.keithl.com/zd_usb.c  
# which contains the current device list as of 2008-Oct-05

# option two ---------------------------------------------------------------
# edit the zd_usb.c file yourself, copying lines from the most recent
# version here:

 http://wireless.kernel.org/download/compat-wireless-2.6/compat-wireless-2.6.tar.bz2

# Untar that into a handy place.  You will be needing the file   zd_usb.c
# to patch the similar file that comes with the kernel.  Otherwise, this
# driver source requires a much more recent kernel.
# 
# copy all the the DEVICE_ZD1211 and DEVICE_ZD1211B lines from the recent
# version   zd_usb.c   and patch them into the kernel source    zd_usb.c

# compile the module, as a normal user --------------------------------------
# using the recipe in   http://wiki.centos.org/HowTos/BuildingKernelModules
     
 cd /usr/src/redhat/BUILD/kernel-2.6.18/linux-2.6.18.i686/
 make clean
 make oldconfig
 make menuconfig     
 make prepare
 make modules_prepare
 make M=drivers/net/wireless/zd1211rw/

 su root
 cp drivers/net/wireless/zd1211rw/*ko /lib/modules/2.6.18-8.1.6.el5/extra/
 /sbin/depmod -a

# all done! ----------------------------------------------------------------

The Wireless USB should now be plug-and-play as eth1.  I did not have
to play with  /etc/sysconfig  or  /etc/modprobe.conf   
Note, this version of the driver, as well as the one running on Ubuntu
8.04 , does not provide signal strength to Network Manager.

The most recent version of this recipe can be found at:
    http://wiki.keithl.com/index.cgi?Zd1211WR

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

> still limiting any single connection to 1.5Mb/s. Is my understanding
> flawed?
>
> --


Bonded T's won't limit you to a single 1.5 connection, you would get a full
3 if they were bonded just like you'd get the full bandwidth of an aggregate
IMA group.

Drew-

Bedroom A has the modem and router.  I wired a dual plate for telephone 
and cat 5.  Bedroom B (on the other side of the wall) I wired the same 
with a wire through the wall.  My research showed two wiring 
possibilities for cat 5 (A & B).  B seemed to be compatible with most 
anything I could buy at the store, so I went with that.  The original 
install through the wall A to B worked no problem for several months.

I then added a wire up through the wall, across the attic, and down 
another wall in the living room.  Wired exactly the same.  This is where 
things went downhill.

Bedroom A works as a feed point and the living room outlet work when 
hooked to my laptop.  Each time I have respliced, replaced wire, or 
replaced the plug at the end, bedroom B doesn't work.

I have all eight wires poked down into the plug per the color code on 
the side of the plug.  I bought Leviton plugs and wall plates at Home 
Depot.  The through the wall wire is appx. 2' long.  The living room is 
about 60' of wire away.

Bruce

Cat 5 needs to be point to point.  You cannot share a drop between
machine and you
cannot have a drop or splice that has a connection point in the middle.
two computers or pieces of equipment two sets of wires
3 - 3
etc.


From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Oct  9 21:44:16 2008
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 9 Oct 2008 21:44:16 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat 5e wiring
In-Reply-To: <ca5165530810092117w52303d3cga03aa938890dd0c8@mail.gmail.com>
References: <48EE97EA.5010609@verizon.net> <48EEAAD8.9010706@gmail.com>
	<48EEBDDC.1010307@verizon.net>
	<ca5165530810092117w52303d3cga03aa938890dd0c8@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <20081009214416.cc270d88.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Thu, 9 Oct 2008 21:17:51 -0700
"Larry Brigman" <larry.brigman at gmail.com> dijo:

> Cat 5 needs to be point to point.  You cannot share a drop between
> machine and you
> cannot have a drop or splice that has a connection point in the middle.
> two computers or pieces of equipment two sets of wires
> 3 - 3
> etc.

Here is what I did (using Cat6, but same principles): I wanted three
outlets in each bedroom, but realized that I would never use more than
one of them. The reason for three wall plates is because I hate cables
snaking across the floor, so I wanted one plate on each of three walls. 

I wired the cable to the plate that was the most likely to be used and
terminated it in a Leviton plug. For purposes of this discussion, call
this plug A. Then I ran a wire from that wall outlet to the next one
(plug B) and just left the wire inside the boxes for plugs A and B. I
put a connector on the cable in both box A and B and also a snap
connector. I repeated the process for plug C. For future reference I
wrote down what I had done on a piece of paper, in triplicate, folded
them, and put a copy inside each box. 

If I ever plug a computer into B or C and it doesn't work, I know the
first thing I'm going to do is pull the plate off the box and inspect
what's inside the box. That's when I'll see the paper, realize that I
need to open all three boxes and use the snap connectors to make the
wire terminate at the plate I want to use.

Kind of clunky, and the couplers may degrade the signal slightly
(although they are rated Cat6), but it works. And chances are I may
never use plates B and C. I just wanted them there because tearing the
sheetrock down to rewire things is way messy.

Bruce KIlpatrick wrote:

>> Bruce KIlpatrick wrote:

> connected together.   One feeds a bedroom and one runs to the living 
> room.  So, I came from a wall plug (feed point), I then took three cable

>

>   
Bruce KIlpatrick
Thank you - yes, that is clear and actually what I feared you might mean.
First, I am not an expert on the matter, so of course I may be wrong.
What I have been given to understand by a cabling pro that I have always 
found to be very reliable and sound, is that one cannot splice cat5 cable.
    At the very least it will no longer perform at cat5 levels.
    At the worst it will not perform at all.
In the old cat3 days (telephony), we used to use factory pressed M/F/F 
splitters and F/F connectors to do the sort of thing you described, but 
I have never seen anything similar for cat5.
Sorry - as I said, I could be wrong.

I shan't pretend I clearly understand the topology, but would it be 
possible to introduce a hub into the mix (something small)?  Would that 
help?

In case it helps ... in our house, the cable comes in through the 
vendor's connection (use to be a cable modem, but now it is an RJ45 wall 
jack from Verizon's FiOS), and into a router/firewall/switch.  From 
there we cable separately to each computer and printer that we need.  
The router/firewall/switch takes a public DHCP IP from the vendor (ISP), 
and it serves up private DHCP IPs internally (192.168.1.x).  The 
printers are configured with static IP's in the middle of the private IP 
range, as the router/firewall/switch (depending on make model and 
configuration) will normally serve up IPs from one end or the other of 
the range, an we certainly don't have enough devices to reach the middle 
- thus we avoid IP conflicts.
Regards
Fred James

On Oct 9, 2008, at 4:46 PM, Bruce KIlpatrick wrote:

> All,
>
> I am trying to run cat 5 cable from my router to two other rooms.  I
> have the wire pulled, spliced together, and am having trouble making
> both cables work.  I have tried resplicing, new wire, and new ends for

Others have chimed in, I have this to say:
"cat 5 requires a six inch bend radius"

When trying to get cables to work, a lot of us older folks are used  
to, well, older rules.

Sure, a two inch bend radius works on our car.

Yeah, we could be sloppy as heck with bell wire, splice it, join it  
with electrical tape, whatever.

Back in 10-T days, it didn't matter if we were TIA-568A, or TIA-568B.

...but *now* it matters.

-Bop

To feed more than one device requires a switch.

---[router]---[computer]

becomes

---[router]---[switch]---[computer]
                      |
                [computer]

From admin at marcscomputersolutions.com  Fri Oct 10 12:20:32 2008
From: admin at marcscomputersolutions.com (Marc Wolsiffer)
Date: Fri, 10 Oct 2008 12:20:32 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat 5e wiring
In-Reply-To: <48EE97EA.5010609@verizon.net>
References: <48EE97EA.5010609@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <000101c92b0d$4443c0f0$cccb42d0$@com>

you didn't accidently stick a loop back anywhere did you? Perhaps you should
stick with wireless next time, haha. Anyways I want to clear some stuff up
with a little bit of education, keep everything T586A or T586B there both
the same line just different ties but can make for a big mess if they get
mixed up. And your spice (running in parallel circuit) cause a amperage
issue in the CAT line. if only one is hooked up it will be fine but 2 or
more and you "could" fry a network card or a router. I don't think 100' will
matter as far as impedance you just might run at 10mb/s but who cares? A
spice should ONLY exist in the form of a hub, switch, and maybe a router. 
Extending a wire should use a patch panel. Clamps and soldering "can" be
used to extend a wire but be very careful because it can send you impedance
through the roof and cause packets to be dropped like crazy. I could get
into detail about frequency and different metals but just trust me, buy a
longer wire!! :)  

I think your relating computer cable and phones too close. phone work on 1
line, the computer work on 2 lines one is only incoming and the other is
only outgoing. So it's like having 2 very hyperactive teenage girls talking
to you on the phone but are unaware that the other one is talking to you.
They can only hear what you say, not what the other one is saying... could
you give them an individual IP? I doubt it ^_^ they would get confused and
keep chatting away. A switch would act like a device that divides them on
the line so you can turn one signal off to talk to teen1 and say your
192.168.1.101 and then "SWITCH" to talk to teen2 and say your 192.168.1.102
so when teen1 says MapQuest and teen2 says Google! You can actually tell
them where to go without getting confused.... Yes I did relate a computer to
a hyperactive teenage girl *gasp* they really don't shut up >_<

Computers can talk at 800,000,000 bits/sec so any static even maybe things
you can't hear with your ear can cause a huge problem in a line for a
computer. So naturally computers need to have a higher level of quality than
a phone. 

I hope this helps, and sorry for the long post. Let me know how it goes!

Before I bought the MyEssentials wireless USB adapter, I mail
ordered a couple of brandX ZyDAS adapters, to try out some
other kinds.  One is still on the way from Hong Kong.  The
other arrived today, and works OK.

http://insidecomputer.stores.yahoo.net/8054wiusb20d.html
USB ID: 0ace:1215 ZyDAS
$18.98 including shipping

Unlike the MyEssentials I wrote about a week ago (ID 050d:705c),
this uses one of the older ZyDAS USB IDs and is supported by the
USB ID tables in older (2.6.18) drivers.   However, for my distro
at least (Scientific Linux, a RHEL5 clone), it still needs the
firmware in /lib/firmware/zd1211 , which can be downloaded from:

http://sourceforge.net/project/showfiles.php?group_id=129083

We will see what the $9.98 Hong Kong USB adapter will do. 
It may take a while to get here.

These will be useful at the Linux Clinic on the 19th.

From m0gely at gmail.com  Fri Oct 10 20:07:57 2008
From: m0gely at gmail.com (m0gely)
Date: Fri, 10 Oct 2008 20:07:57 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Cat 5e wiring
In-Reply-To: <48EEBF33.4090707@verizon.net>
References: <48EE97EA.5010609@verizon.net> <48EEACFC.2020707@fredjame.cnc.net>
	<48EEBF33.4090707@verizon.net>
Message-ID: <48F0188D.6090105@gmail.com>

Bruce KIlpatrick wrote:

> I have a plug in one room for a feed point.  I have two other wires all 
> connected together.   One feeds a bedroom and one runs to the living 
> room.  So, I came from a wall plug (feed point), I then took three cable 
> ends and connected three same colored wires (using round smash type 
> connectors like the phone company used), then ran the other end of two 
> cables to different rooms.

Ok, so this isn't like electricity where you can just tie all the colors 
together. Each wire needs to go from a computer, back to the 
switch/hub/router-with-switch/somethinglikethat. Like this:

http://www.guardianhometechnologies.com/img/home-network/home-network-diagram.gif

That diagram doesn't show any walls, but you should get the idea. Wall 
plates are fine, but not in a way that causes two different computers 
(or any network device) to share a cable. You want a wall plate that has 
enough ports for each room you're going to have a cable run to. Like this:

http://www.summitsource.com/images/products/ACA204.jpg

Actually, you can use a shared cable by using the unused pairs but 
that's very VERY hackish and only to be done in extreme circumstance 
where the boss didn't have the foresight to know he was going to put 
that printer there and was too cheap to by a freaking switch. But I 
digress...

-- 
- m0gely

Bruce KIlpatrick
I may be stretching my memory, but didn't you say you had cut a length 
of wire for each leg of the run?
 From that I assume you "punched" the wires down at each terminus and 
juncture, in such a way that at some juncture(s) there might have been 
two (or more) sets of wire punched down on top of each other?
Or was the connection/extension done with with those crimp devices?
Yes, that's probably what you did say, now that I think of it.
No matter - either way is quite iffy.  Iffy enough that it doesn't 
always work in telco, and really iffy in cat5
So that junction (splice?) is the probable cause.
One would need a Cat5 testing set to actually see the problem - they 
usually have some sort of readout or display that tells that sort of 
thing.  Some of them will print you a nice report.
Regards
Fred James

The Zonet ZEW2500P is a small external USB-cabled unit.
Newegg is out of stock, ewiz.com claims to have them for $22.79 
before shipping.  However, when I google for Zonet, I get a "This
site may harm your computer" warning, so I'm not going there. 
According to the Provantage spec page, it transmits at 17dBm
(50mW) in B mode and 13dBm (20mw) in G mode - good for a nearby
access point, less good for a distant one.

I would still like to learn more, though.  Could you please:

1) Tell us what the FCC ID is (should be on a label on the bottom)?

2) Tell us what /sbin/lsusb says the ID is?
   I'm guessing:   148f:2573 Ralink Technology, Corp

3) Extra credit: with /sbin/lsmod, can you figure out which driver
   it uses?  I'm guessing Ralink RT73 .

> [   32.946398] usbcore: registered new interface driver rt2500usb
> [   33.234853] phy1: Selected rate control algorithm 'simple'
> [   33.298839] usbcore: registered new interface driver rt73usb

That is indeed the RT73 driver, usb version.  These drivers are often
built up out of many modules.  You described it just right, and this
will help others who buy the device.

>> 1) Tell us what the FCC ID is (should be on a label on the bottom)?
>
> FCC ID PQP-WU233G

That is useful to others to tell if they got the same product as you -
sometimes the manufacturer will change the guts and the chipset
without changing the product model number.   When the manufacturer
does that, they are required to change the FCC ID, which is why
that is valuable to the rest of the community.  Thank you!


However, if you are an obsessive nerd like me, you can learn all
sorts of additional stuff about your device.  Go to:

https://fjallfoss.fcc.gov/oetcf/eas/reports/GenericSearch.cfm

You can enter "PQP" for the Grantee code, and "-WU233G" as the
product code, and then click Detail to get to this page:

https://fjallfoss.fcc.gov/oetcf/eas/reports/ViewExhibitReport.cfm?mode=Exhibits&RequestTimeout=500&calledFromFrame=N&application_id=395176&fcc_id='PQP-WU233G'

You will need to install the Traditional Chinese Fonts to view
some of the PDFs,  available for free download from:

http://www.adobe.com/products/acrobat/acrrasianfontpack.html

Then you can look at the manuals schematics, pictures of the guts,
etc.  Sometimes the internal pictures are the only way to tell
which exact chip is used - in this case, an RT2572T MAC chip and
an RT2526L transceiver chip.  The 93C66 is a 4Kbit serial memory
that stores the MAC address, calibration information, and the
legal wifi channels for the US.  Hack that, and you can jack up
your power, have your very own wireless band, interfere with all
all sorts of other communication, and get the FCC on your ass :-/ .

Bruce KIlpatrick wrote:

you didn't accidently stick a loop back anywhere did you? Perhaps you should
stick with wireless next time, haha. Anyways I want to clear some stuff up
with a little bit of education,
keep everything T586A or T586B there both the same line just different ties
but can make for a big mess if they get mixed up. And your spice (running in
parallel circuit) cause a amperage issue in the CAT line. if only one is
hooked up it will be fine but 2 or more and you "could" fry a network card
or a router. I don't think 100' will matter as far as impedance you just
might run at 10mb/s but who cares? A spice should ONLY exist in the form of
a hub, switch, and maybe a router. 
Extending a wire should use a patch panel. Clamps and soldering "can" be
used to extend a wire but be very careful because it can send you impedance
through the roof and cause packets to be dropped like crazy. I could get
into detail about frequency and different metals but just trust me, buy a
longer wire!! :)  

I think your relating computer cable and phones too close. phone work on 1
line, the computer work on 2 lines one is only incoming and the other is
only outgoing. So it's like having 2 very hyperactive teenage girls talking
to you on the phone but are unaware that the other one is talking to you.
They can only hear what you say, not what the other one is saying... could
you give them an individual IP? I doubt it ^_^ they would get confused and
keep chatting away. A switch would act like a device that divides them on
the line so you can turn one signal off to talk to teen1 and say your
192.168.1.101 and then "SWITCH" to talk to teen2 and say your 192.168.1.102
so when teen1 says MapQuest and teen2 says Google! You can actually tell
them where to go without getting confused.... Yes I did relate a computer to
a hyperactive teenage girl *gasp* they really don't shut up >_<

Computers can talk at 800,000,000 bits/sec so any static even maybe things
you can't hear with your ear can cause a huge problem in a line for a
computer. So naturally computers need to have a higher level of quality than
a phone. 

I hope this helps, and sorry for the long post. Let me know how it goes!

A followup.

It turns out that the web page should not have told me it was sending me an e-mail because it does so only if you pay for the service. I had the free 6-day trial version. All I had to do was use a command from the terminal using the username and password that I signed up with.

Once you issue the command it starts the service and it just runs as long as the computer is running. Unfortunately, it does not add an entry to the startup scripts, so if you reboot you must reenter the command manually. There has been discussion of this on Memopal's forums, so presumably this will be fixed. Bear in mind that the Linux version of the software is still officially in beta. It appears that eventually there will also be a GUI version of the software.

More problematic is that there is a bug that causes the backup to stop after a while. Again, this is discussed at length on the Memopal forums for Linux, but no one knows why and the Memopal staff do not post anything on the forums. 

As long as it is running it is pretty cool. I notice no slowdown in my upload and download speeds while using the computer. Their software must be doing something rsync-y, except that it saves older versions of files on their server. And $49 for 150 GB for one year (25% discount for each subsequent year) is a pretty good price for the service. I just wish they would say something about when they expect to have the bugs fixed. Because of the sudden stopping I cannot get it to upload more than about 3.2 GB.

>>>>> "Marc" == Marc Wolsiffer <admin at marcscomputersolutions.com> writes:

Russell> Bear in mind, high transmit power does you no good unless the
Russell> thing you are talking to has a similarly high transmit power.
Russell> All the extra tx power does is evaporate more moisture from
Russell> surrounding trees and interfere with your neighbors trying to
Russell> operate on the same (or nearby) channel.

Marc> This is my first post, Remember a 500mw is illegal if you're
Marc> running it in Oregon.  100mw is the legal limit [...]

I think you are wrong.  The limits are established by the FCC.  You
can transmit with 1W with a 6dBi antenna, or combinations of lower
wattage and higher antenna gain.  For point-to-point, you can exceed
that.  Google EIRP.

E.g.:

  <http://www.michwave.com/bbnetwork/faq/fcc.htm>

For example, the SkyPilot access point devices that MetroFi used,
employed 400 mW radios (26 dBm) and 7.5 dBi antennas.  When you add
those gains together, you get 33.5 dBm, so they are at least a few dB
under the limit.  The antenna gain of most USB or pccard type wifi is
more apt to be around 2 dBi, so more tx power (up to a watt) is legal.

Oregon, as far as I know, has no authority to regulate transmit power
in the ISM bands, and has not attempted to do so.  If I am wrong,
please point me to the rule or legislation.

Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Oct 13 00:50:36 2008

Subject: [PLUG] 500mW transmitters (was) USB Wifi - My Essentials...
In-Reply-To: <000001c927fa$fd49a8a0$f7dcf9e0$@com>

	<86od1yg2fq.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>

On Mon, Oct 06, 2008 at 02:32:08PM -0700, Marc Wolsiffer wrote:
> 
> Remember a 500mw is illegal if you're running it in Oregon. 
> 100mw is the legal limit and if found my neighbor saw you with a 500mw
> transmitter you would have investigation going on, she doesn't even hold
> cell phones to her head. The antenna doesn't look like it can support a
> clean 500mw TX anyways. I would go with a directional antenna at a lower TX
> rate and/or a good repeater. DD-wrt can help boot your range a ton.  I set
> have set-up government wifi solutions for a while with RADIUS servers and
> splash pages. Don't fry our children :)

I assume you are joking - perhaps if I understood your syntax I 
would understand your point.

1)  The FCC regulates radios, not the states.  If the states tried,
they would probably be sued by the manufacturers, claiming interference
with interstate commerce.  If Oregon does have such a law, please
provide the Oregon Revised Statute number (they are online);  a few
minutes of looking around produced nothing like a limit.

2)  Cell phones can go up to a watt, especially if they are a long
way from, or shielded from, the base station.   And most people
hold that one watt transmitter right next to their head without
melting their ears off.  A 500mW wireless transmitter is usually
much farther away, and the inverse square law applies.

3)  Antennas do not limit power, at least not until they melt from
resistive losses or dielectric absorption.  Sometimes antennas
couple poorly, have a high standing wave ratio, etc., but with
a proper matching network they not reflect power back to the
transmitter and cause distortion.  Wireless devices are sample
tested for spurious emissions, and you can see the results for
particular devices at the FCC website I pointed at in the last
post.  For the "500mW" Alfa (actually 290mW B and and 90mW G )
with FCCID UQ2 AWUS036H :

https://fjallfoss.fcc.gov/prod/oet/forms/blobs/retrieve.cgi?attachment_id=786770&native_or_pdf=pdf

The Alfa is well within the FCC limits on all specs.  The dipole
antenna on the device has a numeric gain of 1.58, and that short
antenna an efficient emitter at 2.4GHz.  

4)  Again, the main issue with excessive power wifi (as Russell noted
so well) is that your packets are competing with other packets for
the attention of all the other access points you *aren't* talking
to.  Rude and unneighborly.  The additional power won't help you if
the access point you are listening to is wimpy and you can't hear it. 
If there is only one access point around, and it has decent power,
higher power might improve your connection. 

I would only use the Alfa rather than my 100mW Netgear WG511T
if I had trouble reaching the access point.  If nothing else, the
Alfa probably drains my laptop battery faster than the WG511T.

5)  If 500mW transmitters can fry your children, your species is
very small ...

Keith

hmm, i wouldn't use du to do it, but i think the root of your problem
is that you're using the "-h" flag which flubs the sorting-by-number
system up, since 1008K > 100M when you start letting computers sort
naively.

i'd probably do find . -type f -size +50M -exec ls -l {} \; |sort -r
-n -k +5 | head

Circa 13:54:52 on Mon, 13 Oct 2008, Fred James wrote: 
> chris (fool) mccraw wrote:
> > On Mon, Oct 13, 2008 at 13:24, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> >   
> >> This ought to be a piece of cake, but I can't get it to work.
[...snip...]

Long ago Randal published the following perl script which does the job
handily...

[sherring at pisces ~]$ cat scripts/findbig
#!/usr/bin/perl
require 'getopts.pl';
require 'find.pl';
&Getopts('n:');
$n = defined $opt_n ? $opt_n : 20;
@ARGV = ($ENV{'HOME'}) unless @ARGV;
&find(@ARGV);
sub wanted {
        $size{$name} = -s _ if not -l and -f _;
}
@sorted = sort { $size{$b} <=> $size{$a} } keys %size;
for (@sorted[0..($n-1)]) {
        printf
            "%40s, %5dK, last accessed %3d days ago\n",
            $_ , (-s)/1024, -A _;
}

e.g.

[sherring at pisces ~]$ findbig n10 /var
                /var/spool/mail/sherring, 382975K, last accessed   0 days ago
                   /var/lib/rpm/Packages, 49892K, last accessed   0 days ago
                   /var/lib/rpm/Filemd5s, 10160K, last accessed  12 days ago
                        /var/log/lastlog,  6454K, last accessed   0 days ago
/var/cache/yum/base/primary.xml.gz.sqlite,  5956K, last accessed   9 days ago
                  /var/lib/rpm/Basenames,  5532K, last accessed  12 days ago
                   /var/lib/rpm/Dirnames,  3084K, last accessed  12 days ago
     /var/www/html/gallery-backup.tar.gz,  2050K, last accessed 980 days ago

   This morning I received a call from Verizon Wireless's Fraud Department.
The encryption chip(?) in my Treo 700p is apparently weak or failing and
someone was able to grab the radio signal and enough information that they
could clone the phone. It was used from New York City to call the Dominican
Republic Friday and Saturday. The most likely location for intercepting the
radio signal was at Chicago's O'Hare Airport Thursday evening while I changed
planes.

   Palm has a firmware upgrade to the 700p that fixes the problem, along with
some other issues. However, the upgrade can be applied _only_ via computers
running WinXP, W2K, or Mac OS X with Palm's desktop software installed. They
have _no_ linux support whatsoever. This bothers me as I've used Palm OS
PDAs and Smartphones since 1995 or so, and with linux (jpilot) since 1997.
So, no fix from Palm.

   Verizon Wireless's fraud busters were able to talk me through changing the
encryption codes so they could turn outgoing call capability from my phone
number back on. However, this does not upgrade the firmware, only let's them
block all calls made from a phone claiming to be mine, but with the old
code.

   I don't need a replacement right away since the Treo 700p is only about a
year old. But, I would like to learn what linux-supporting smartphones (pda
and telephone is all I need; no music, pictures, TV, or anything else
needed). I've nothing against Verizon Wireless (they were very helpful today
as they have been ever since I bought cellular service with AirTouch), but
they do charge $60 per month for 'Net access (Web only, I believe) which I
wouldn't use enough to justify the cost.

   Please suggest such a cell phone and share your experiences with it.
Compatibility with pilot-link and jpilot is almost a necessity.

i've been using a pilot since '98 (about 6 palm III/IIIx's over the
course of 8 years and a treo 700p now) and unfortunately they've
always suffered this problem--the rom update utility was always a
windows or mac utility.  the one time every two years (more in the
middle, when i was going through used palm III's at the rate of one
per 8 months) that i had to upgrade the ROM, i always needed a mac or
win machine to do so. as in 1998, so in 2008.  there was never a
*need*, strictly speaking (as you see, your phone still works..) but i
do like the later revs of palmOS esp on the old kit.  as you say,
everything else for the pilot is better under linux--i have fixed more
than one horked device by doing something like dumping and restoring
all the memos or contacts after some minor edits, for instance.  i
love being able to back everything up with a single command, and
restore to another device with absolutely no hassle.  the two or three
times i really tried to use palm's windows or mac software to do
synchronization, it was so un-unix-like that i felt like an imbecile
and screwed things up thoroughly.  pilot-link Just Works, and is
intuitive.

From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Tue Oct 14 10:04:42 2008
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: Tue, 14 Oct 2008 10:04:42 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] List ten biggest files with du
References: <20081013132442.1607f1c5.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<ae2db4620810131339k35689f53xfa5f6b6cbeaad045@mail.gmail.com>
	<48F3B59C.5060504@fredjame.cnc.net>
	<20081013210321.GA6597@pickled.herring.org>
Message-ID: <86vdvvf6qd.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "Sandy" == Sandy Herring <sandy at herring.org> writes:

Sandy> require 'find.pl';

That is *ancient*.  A modern one with File::Find would look like:

    my $N = 10; # ok, so it's not a command line arg :)

    use File::Find;

    my %size;
    find(sub {
      $size{$File::Find::name} = -s _ if not -l and -f _;
    }, @ARGV);
    @sorted = sort { $size{$b} <=> $size{$a} } keys %size;
    for (@sorted[0..($n-1)]) {
      printf
        "%40s, %5dK, last accessed %3d days ago\n",
        $_ , (-s)/1024, -A _;
    }

Untested, but I often get this right.

I've been keeping a watch on the Google Android, rolling out with
Tmobile sometime in the next few months.  That will be open-source
everything.   http://code.google.com/android/

I am even less of a power user than you, but I expect the open source
community will come up with some interesting oddball applications for
Android.   Personally, I'm interested in intercepting the sound signal
path, and doing some digital signal processing on it.  I am hard of
hearing, and perhaps I can improve sound quality, or adapt the sound
level to ambient noise, or otherwise make mobile phones a little more
usable for me.  If I am local, I might even route the sound to my
home server and do some speech recognition on it, sending back the
text in near-real-time if possible.

I also expect to be able to hack hardware into it;  for example, a
belt-loop recharging cradle, or a better headset, or ...

My wife is a doctor and PDA user.  I can imagine quite a few more 
apps being useful to her, and this device becoming her interface for
everything - pager (via internet), dictation + text, patient chart
notes, web searches, etc.  Being a unix-like machine, I hope we can
set it up super-securely, back it up with rsync, etc.  

All that will depend on an active development community, of course. 
My wife and I will probably limp along on our brick phones until we
see how the early adopters fare.  When some of my friends start buying
these I will be very interested in helping with the learning process.

Keith

> That is *ancient*.  A modern one with File::Find would look like:
> 
>     my $N = 10; # ok, so it's not a command line arg :)
> 
>     use File::Find;
> 
>     my %size;
>     find(sub {
>       $size{$File::Find::name} = -s _ if not -l and -f _;
>     }, @ARGV);
>     @sorted = sort { $size{$b} <=> $size{$a} } keys %size;
>     for (@sorted[0..($n-1)]) {
>       printf
>         "%40s, %5dK, last accessed %3d days ago\n",
>         $_ , (-s)/1024, -A _;
>     }
> 
> Untested, but I often get this right.

the n in the for loop needs to be uppercased - then it work.

Thanks!
Sandy

From alan at clueserver.org  Tue Oct 14 11:46:31 2008
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)

Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCEMENT: PLUG Advanced Topics meeting October 15th 2008

PLUG Advanced Topics Meeting
October 15th 2008 
7pm - 9pm
Jax Bar
826 SW 2nd Ave
Portland, OR 97204
(503) 228-9128

Speaker:   Robert Love
           Intel

Subject: Open FibreChannel over Ethernet

Robert Love is a Sr. Software Engineer at Intel Corporation. He
graduated from The University of Maryland, College Park with a B.S. in
Computer Science and has worked at Intel for the last 7 years. Most of
that time was spent writing server management software, but lately his
focus has shifted to networked storage solutions. Robert was involved in
the enabling of iSCSI boot technology for Linux but is now focused on
Fibre Channel over Ethernet. In November of 2007, Robert published a GPL
FCoE solution with the intent of merging it into the Linux kernel. He,
and his team, continue to evolve that initial work in the pursuit of
Linux kernel inclusion.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Oct 14 16:34:27 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 14 Oct 2008 16:34:27 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Taiwan wireless USB device arrives, works fine
Message-ID: <20081014233427.GA7908@gate.kl-ic.com>


I am connected through wireless USB adapter I bought from "yyhpeter"
through Ebay, item number 350104540187 , a buy-it-now auction running
through the end of October.   $9.99, free shipping.  The manufacturer
is Safe Home, www.safehome.com.tw

Suggested, for those that want inexpensive and Linux compatable
wireless adapters.  Shipping from Taiwan is about two weeks.  It is
only a 20mW transmitter, so it does not have much range, but it should
work with nearby access points.  

/sbin/lsusb:  0ace:1215 ZyDAS

... which according to the driver source, is a ZyDAS ZD1211B .  This
is an older USB ID, and will be in the built in drivers back to the
2.6.18 kernels (some newer IDs, with the same chipset, are only included
in newer versions of the driver).  For some distros, you must download
the firmware to go into the /lib/firmware/zd1211 directory, which you
can get from here:

http://sourceforge.net/project/showfiles.php?group_id=129083&package_id=187875

The FCC ID is PAN WL2203V .  Reports available here:

https://fjallfoss.fcc.gov/oetcf/eas/reports/ViewExhibitReport.cfm?mode=Exhibits&RequestTimeout=500&calledFromFrame=N&application_id=217044&fcc_id='PANWL2203V'

If you want a bit more range,  the MyEssentials wireless USB adapter
($25 at Fry's) also uses the ZD1211B but has a 50mW transmitter.
With a more recent USB ID, it may require a driver upgrade.

I will be bringing both to the Clinic on Sunday.  If people like
the Safe Home device, perhaps you can get together to make a group
buy and get some even cheaper.  Then, perhaps we can stop dealing
with adapters that don't work with Linux.

Keith

From creswick at gmail.com  Tue Oct 14 16:44:38 2008

On Tue, Oct 14, 2008 at 10:39 AM, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
> I've been keeping a watch on the Google Android, rolling out with
> Tmobile sometime in the next few months.  That will be open-source
> everything.   http://code.google.com/android/

So, I've been looking into this more, and I was initially leaning
towards the OpenMoko Freerunner, since it seems to offer the best of
both worlds: Open software and Open hardware (android is just a
software spec that works on a number of phones, from what I
understand).  I was also under the impression that, since Android is
Open Source (Apache licensed, even) that Open Moko hardware would be
able to run Android.

Alas, Android is *not* "all open".  The layer that actually talks to
the hardware is proprietary, and as far as I have been able to tell,
there are no plans to open source that portion.  Even given that, it
would *probably* work on OpenMoko, but the builds were created for a
different version of the ARM instruction set.

Blog posts about this: (the first links to the second)

http://jyro.blogspot.com/2008/01/openmoko-android-and-great-hacking-post.html
http://benno.id.au/blog/2007/11/21/android-neo1973

talking to devs in the openmoko irc channel seems to confirm that this
is still relevant.

This "semi-open" structure, combined with the restriction of carries
on the G1 is probably going to be enough to offset the higher cost of
the Freerunner, IMO.

(Besides, the Freerunner currently has three different software stacks
you can run, and at least one of them is based on Enlightenment Dr17!)

--Rogan

>
> I am even less of a power user than you, but I expect the open source
> community will come up with some interesting oddball applications for
> Android.   Personally, I'm interested in intercepting the sound signal
> path, and doing some digital signal processing on it.  I am hard of
> hearing, and perhaps I can improve sound quality, or adapt the sound
> level to ambient noise, or otherwise make mobile phones a little more
> usable for me.  If I am local, I might even route the sound to my
> home server and do some speech recognition on it, sending back the
> text in near-real-time if possible.

> belt-loop recharging cradle, or a better headset, or ...

HOT, and the projector needs to keep running the fan and cooling the
bulb after the light shuts off, or else the insides get toasted.

> desktop software to be installed on someone's machine, and that's apparently
> necessary. The Verizon stores cannot do the Palm upgrade, only Verizon
> stuff.

You don't need the software and Win/OSX isn't required. You just need 
the SD card and a way to get data on it. It sounds like this is what you 
need to do:

http://www.palm.com/us/support/downloads/treo700pupdate/verizon_installsd.html

Get the file and use your Linux software to put the file on the Fat32 
formatted SD card while in the phone and connected to your PC. You can 
get a 1GB card for ~$10 or less or borrow one from a camera you have 
possibly, or a friend.

-- 
- m0gely


From raanders at acm.org  Wed Oct 15 08:05:45 2008
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)
Date: Wed, 15 Oct 2008 08:05:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Actions after a USB device is plugged in
Message-ID: <48F606C9.40901@acm.org>

A HAM radio operator has a packet control device(?) whose Windows 
software runs under Wine.  His runs Ubuntu.

When he plugs a the USB-to-serial device the systems mounts(?) it as 
/dev/ttyUSB0.  The problem comes because the Windows software will only 
see /dev/ttyS[0-3] (aka. COM1 - COM4).

We were able to soft link and get the software to see and use it but it 
appears Ubuntu cleans out the /dev directory entries either during 
shutdown, startup, or when the USB-to-serial device is removed.  The 
link didn't last between reboots.  So for now he opens a terminal, sudo 
to root, and creates the link each time he boots.

Is there a way to automagically run a script or command (ln -sf 
/dev/ttyUSB0 /dev/ttyS3) after the USB-to-serial device is plugged in?

I don't run Ubuntu and can't install it as my bench/test machine is 
being used by my wife while I fix her system. 8-(

Any help is appreciated.


Thanks,
Rod
--

Rod,

   It ain't unique to Ubuntu. It took me a while to grok the differences when
I switched from my Kyocera smartphone and its serial charging/data
connection to the Treo with its USB data connection.

   The USB connection is created when the device is attached and the button
on the cable is pressed, and destroyed when the device is removed.
Unfortunately, softlinks fail because they point to a specific /dev/ttUSB.
Here's a session from /var/log/messages:

Oct 15 08:47:42 salmo kernel: usb 1-1: new full speed USB device using
ohci_hcd and address 65
Oct 15 08:47:42 salmo kernel: usb 1-1: configuration #1 chosen from 1 choice
Oct 15 08:47:42 salmo kernel: visor 1-1:1.0: Handspring Visor / Palm OS
converter detected
Oct 15 08:47:42 salmo kernel: usb 1-1: Handspring Visor / Palm OS converter
now attached to ttyUSB0
Oct 15 08:47:42 salmo kernel: usb 1-1: Handspring Visor / Palm OS converter
now attached to ttyUSB1
Oct 15 08:47:43 salmo kernel: usb 1-1: USB disconnect, address 65
Oct 15 08:47:43 salmo kernel: visor ttyUSB0: Handspring Visor / Palm OS
converter now disconnected from ttyUSB0
Oct 15 08:47:43 salmo kernel: visor ttyUSB1: Handspring Visor / Palm OS
converter now disconnected from ttyUSB1
Oct 15 08:47:43 salmo kernel: visor 1-1:1.0: device disconnected

   Why it always generates two devices I don't know. Perhaps each one is
monodirectional.

> Is there a way to automagically run a script or command (ln -sf
> /dev/ttyUSB0 /dev/ttyS3) after the USB-to-serial device is plugged in?

   The answer to this is above my pay grade; I don't know. I do specify
/dev/tts/USB1 in the jpilot port setting and it seems to Just Work.

Rich

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Wed Oct 15 08:57:02 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Wed, 15 Oct 2008 08:57:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Actions after a USB device is plugged in
In-Reply-To: <48F606C9.40901@acm.org>
References: <48F606C9.40901@acm.org>
Message-ID: <20081015155702.GG2502@sentinelchicken.org>

> Is there a way to automagically run a script or command (ln -sf 
> /dev/ttyUSB0 /dev/ttyS3) after the USB-to-serial device is plugged in?
> 
> I don't run Ubuntu and can't install it as my bench/test machine is 
> being used by my wife while I fix her system. 8-(
> 
> Any help is appreciated.

Most modern Linux distributions use a daemon called udev or something
similar to automatically generate /dev devices at boot time and for
hotplugging events (like USB devices being added/removed).  Your best
bet will probably be to look into udev's configuration under /etc/udev
and create a rule to set up that symlink when the specific device is
detected in the system.

HTH,
tim

There is nothing preventing you from creating a symlink when the target
does not exist.  It will be a broken link until the target becomes
available.  Why not just write an init.d script that creates the link?

> There is nothing preventing you from creating a symlink when the target
> does not exist.  It will be a broken link until the target becomes
> available.  Why not just write an init.d script that creates the link?

Well, because the specific device being set up is not guaranteed to be
mapped to /dev/ttyUSB0.  Maybe that's the only tty USB device that
system will ever see, but udev is desiged to create these mappings more
correctly, from what I understand.

tim

Eric,

   It's not .bash_profile, but a permissions problem. If I log on a different
console as root the keys are remapped. Logged in as me I'm told I don't have
permissions for the keymaps 0, 1, and 2.

   These appear to be in /usr/share/kbd/, and all directories are world
executable (755) while owned by root.root. The individual i386/querty/*
files are world readable (644) so I don't know where the permission error
originates. The perms from / down match on the workstation/server and the
notebook.

   You've shown me where the problem originates, and I appreciate that. Can
you now suggest how I can determine where the permissions problem is?

Thanks very much,

> Eric,
>
>   It's not .bash_profile, but a permissions problem. If I log on a different
> console as root the keys are remapped. Logged in as me I'm told I don't have
> permissions for the keymaps 0, 1, and 2.
>
>   These appear to be in /usr/share/kbd/, and all directories are world
> executable (755) while owned by root.root. The individual i386/querty/*
> files are world readable (644) so I don't know where the permission error
> originates. The perms from / down match on the workstation/server and the
> notebook.
>
>   You've shown me where the problem originates, and I appreciate that. Can
> you now suggest how I can determine where the permissions problem is?

I'd use strace on a manual loadkeys run.  It'll show you exactly which
call is creating the error, and if it's in reference to a file (tty
ownership?) you can perhaps correct it via udev (if udev is in use) or
just chmod/chowning the device files permanently otherwise.

If it's a syscall not referring to a file for which you don't have
permission, post the call and the error here and we'll figure out how
to change the situation.

> Eric,
>
>   It's not .bash_profile, but a permissions problem. If I log on a
> different
> console as root the keys are remapped. Logged in as me I'm told I don't
> have
> permissions for the keymaps 0, 1, and 2.
>
>   These appear to be in /usr/share/kbd/, and all directories are world
> executable (755) while owned by root.root. The individual i386/querty/*
> files are world readable (644) so I don't know where the permission error
> originates. The perms from / down match on the workstation/server and the
> notebook.
>
>   You've shown me where the problem originates, and I appreciate that. Can
> you now suggest how I can determine where the permissions problem is?
>
> Thanks very much,
>
> Rich
>

You could try strace. Just put strace in front of the full command you use
to try to change key maps, and look at the last few lines of output.

You may have to install the strace utility if your distro doesn't come with
it by default.

-wes

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Wed Oct 15 11:51:04 2008
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Wed, 15 Oct 2008 12:51:04 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Keymap not being followed on console

> On Wed, 15 Oct 2008, chris (fool) mccraw wrote:

>> call is creating the error, and if it's in reference to a file (tty
>> ownership?) you can perhaps correct it via udev (if udev is in use) or
>> just chmod/chowning the device files permanently otherwise.

>   It appears to be KDSKBENT. A Google search turns up 2,640 hits from
> different distributions, mostly Red Hat-ish. That string is followed by "-1
> EPERM (Operation not permitted)".

> Rich

On the ol' gentoo laptop, this is what I find when grepping the kernel
sources:

root at sphinktop:/usr/src/linux# find -type f -print0 |xargs -0 -e grep -n -i 'KDSKBENT'
./fs/compat_ioctl.c:2011:COMPATIBLE_IOCTL(KDSKBENT)
./drivers/char/vt_ioctl.c:96:   case KDSKBENT:
./drivers/char/vt_ioctl.c:576:  case KDSKBENT:
./drivers/s390/char/keyboard.c:362:     case KDSKBENT:
./drivers/s390/char/keyboard.c:478:     case KDSKBENT:
./security/selinux/hooks.c:2839:                case KDSKBENT:
./include/linux/kd.h:109:#define KDSKBENT       0x4B47  /* sets one entry in translation table */

keyboard.c seems a likely place to start looking.  There are these two
functions in there.  Notice that the first one calls the second one, and
the second one returns -EPERM.  The key line seems to be this:

     perm = current->signal->tty == kbd->tty || capable(CAP_SYS_TTY_CONFIG);

If this is false, you get your error.

What does this all mean?  No idea.



int kbd_ioctl(struct kbd_data *kbd, struct file *file,
       unsigned int cmd, unsigned long arg)
{
     void __user *argp;
     int ct, perm;

     argp = (void __user *)arg;

     /*
      * To have permissions to do most of the vt ioctls, we either have
      * to be the owner of the tty, or have CAP_SYS_TTY_CONFIG.
      */
     perm = current->signal->tty == kbd->tty || capable(CAP_SYS_TTY_CONFIG);
     switch (cmd) {
     case KDGKBTYPE:
         return put_user(KB_101, (char __user *)argp);
     case KDGKBENT:
     case KDSKBENT:
         return do_kdsk_ioctl(kbd, argp, cmd, perm);
     case KDGKBSENT:
     case KDSKBSENT:
         return do_kdgkb_ioctl(kbd, argp, cmd, perm);
     case KDGKBDIACR:
     {
         struct kbdiacrs __user *a = argp;
         struct kbdiacr diacr;
         int i;

         if (put_user(kbd->accent_table_size, &a->kb_cnt))
             return -EFAULT;
         for (i = 0; i < kbd->accent_table_size; i++) {
             diacr.diacr = kbd->accent_table[i].diacr;
             diacr.base = kbd->accent_table[i].base;
             diacr.result = kbd->accent_table[i].result;
             if (copy_to_user(a->kbdiacr + i, &diacr, sizeof(struct kbdiacr)))
             return -EFAULT;
         }
         return 0;
     }
     case KDGKBDIACRUC:
     {
         struct kbdiacrsuc __user *a = argp;

         ct = kbd->accent_table_size;
         if (put_user(ct, &a->kb_cnt))
             return -EFAULT;
         if (copy_to_user(a->kbdiacruc, kbd->accent_table,
                  ct * sizeof(struct kbdiacruc)))
             return -EFAULT;
         return 0;
     }
     case KDSKBDIACR:
     {
         struct kbdiacrs __user *a = argp;
         struct kbdiacr diacr;
         int i;

         if (!perm)
             return -EPERM;
         if (get_user(ct, &a->kb_cnt))
             return -EFAULT;
         if (ct >= MAX_DIACR)
             return -EINVAL;
         kbd->accent_table_size = ct;
         for (i = 0; i < ct; i++) {
             if (copy_from_user(&diacr, a->kbdiacr + i, sizeof(struct kbdiacr)))
                 return -EFAULT;
             kbd->accent_table[i].diacr = diacr.diacr;
             kbd->accent_table[i].base = diacr.base;
             kbd->accent_table[i].result = diacr.result;
         }
         return 0;
     }
     case KDSKBDIACRUC:
     {
         struct kbdiacrsuc __user *a = argp;

         if (!perm)
             return -EPERM;
         if (get_user(ct, &a->kb_cnt))
             return -EFAULT;
         if (ct >= MAX_DIACR)
             return -EINVAL;
         kbd->accent_table_size = ct;
         if (copy_from_user(kbd->accent_table, a->kbdiacruc,
                    ct * sizeof(struct kbdiacruc)))
             return -EFAULT;
         return 0;
     }
     default:
         return -ENOIOCTLCMD;
     }
}



/*
  * Ioctl stuff.
  */
static int
do_kdsk_ioctl(struct kbd_data *kbd, struct kbentry __user *user_kbe,
           int cmd, int perm)
{
     struct kbentry tmp;
     ushort *key_map, val, ov;

     if (copy_from_user(&tmp, user_kbe, sizeof(struct kbentry)))
         return -EFAULT;
#if NR_KEYS < 256
     if (tmp.kb_index >= NR_KEYS)
         return -EINVAL;
#endif
#if MAX_NR_KEYMAPS < 256
     if (tmp.kb_table >= MAX_NR_KEYMAPS)
         return -EINVAL;
#endif

     switch (cmd) {
     case KDGKBENT:
         key_map = kbd->key_maps[tmp.kb_table];
         if (key_map) {
             val = U(key_map[tmp.kb_index]);
             if (KTYP(val) >= KBD_NR_TYPES)
             val = K_HOLE;
         } else
             val = (tmp.kb_index ? K_HOLE : K_NOSUCHMAP);
         return put_user(val, &user_kbe->kb_value);
     case KDSKBENT:
         if (!perm)
             return -EPERM;
         if (!tmp.kb_index && tmp.kb_value == K_NOSUCHMAP) {
             /* disallocate map */
             key_map = kbd->key_maps[tmp.kb_table];
             if (key_map) {
                 kbd->key_maps[tmp.kb_table] = NULL;
                 kfree(key_map);
             }
             break;
         }

         if (KTYP(tmp.kb_value) >= KBD_NR_TYPES)
             return -EINVAL;
         if (KVAL(tmp.kb_value) > kbd_max_vals[KTYP(tmp.kb_value)])
             return -EINVAL;

         if (!(key_map = kbd->key_maps[tmp.kb_table])) {
             int j;

             key_map = kmalloc(sizeof(plain_map),
                              GFP_KERNEL);
             if (!key_map)
                 return -ENOMEM;
             kbd->key_maps[tmp.kb_table] = key_map;
             for (j = 0; j < NR_KEYS; j++)
                 key_map[j] = U(K_HOLE);
         }
         ov = U(key_map[tmp.kb_index]);
         if (tmp.kb_value == ov)
             break;  /* nothing to do */
         /*
          * Attention Key.
          */
         if (((ov == K_SAK) || (tmp.kb_value == K_SAK)) &&
             !capable(CAP_SYS_ADMIN))
             return -EPERM;
         key_map[tmp.kb_index] = U(tmp.kb_value);
         break;
     }
     return 0;
}

Carlos

to be a suspect in this case).

> If you're really curious, you could just run `strings' against it. That
> won't tell you how it got onto your computer, but it may clear up the

Much of the time malware authors will ofuscate the binary with some sort
of compression utility.  The file command will reveal may of these
executable compressors.

I recently encountered a malware program designed as a rar archive
password program.  If you ran it, it installed a nasty little trojan on
your system.  (It was included with passworded rar archives of video
files.)  The script kiddie who wrote it did not use any sort of
offuscation.  The password and the name of the malware were in the
binary in clear text.  Running strings would show where he replaced the
defaults.  (It had a "password = foo" and "install = trojan.exe" line in
it.)  Pretty damn pathetic.

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Oct 15 16:01:51 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 15 Oct 2008 16:01:51 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ADBEFLPRCS3_WWE.exe

Message-ID: <c834ec80810151601g8129104kbaf1788d497b4f27@mail.gmail.com>

On Wed, Oct 15, 2008 at 3:46 PM, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
>
> Y'all have me at a disadvantage. I do not know what "running strings" means.
>

'strings' is a program that searches it's argument(s) for printable
characters.  Almost every file has some ASCII-encoded content that's
still ascii-encoded, such as copyright notices, window titles, version
numbers, etc.  Often the entire content of the Help->About window will
be tucked away in a binary this way.  strings will pull that out so
you can see it.  eg:

$ strings ADBEFLPRCS3_WWE.exe

--Rogan

From m0gely at gmail.com  Wed Oct 15 18:07:12 2008
From: m0gely at gmail.com (m0gely)

Message-ID: <48F693C0.4020009@gmail.com>

Rich Shepard wrote:

>    I assume that's a ScanDisk memory card, yes? I think that I have one for
> my old Olympus D-340L camera.

'SanDisk' is one of many brands. 'SD' is the type of flash memory card 
that fits in your phone. Many cameras also use that type of memory. Pay 
attention to the top part in that link. You cannot use the newer type of 
SDHC or MiniSD card even thought they often times come with an adapter 
and will work for your phone under normal conditions. For flashing, it 
must be a plain SD card formatted with the Fat32 (not Fat) file system. 
If your Olympus camera is a few years old then it most likely has the 
type of SD card you need.

-- 
- m0gely

Subject: [PLUG] ADBEFLPRCS3_WWE.exe
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80810151601g8129104kbaf1788d497b4f27@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20081013145552.d010faa6.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<48F55D2D.5030102@gmail.com>	<20081014212229.07ab85d7.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<da2207740810142150q58226470qa0c49a8f8b334c60@mail.gmail.com>
	<20081014230107.e3dd57f2.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<20081015180811.GA6380@pickled.herring.org>
	<1224109417.11841.16.camel@rotwang>
	<20081015154621.7688de23.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<c834ec80810151601g8129104kbaf1788d497b4f27@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <48F69FBA.9030703@verizon.net>

Even if you don't find anything I'd be very hesitant to install that 
program if you found it semi-randomly off the internet. I believe it's 
been mentioned thats the installer for adobe flash (not the flash player 
but the flash producer), I happen to know from doing lots of installs of 
adobe software at work that ADBEFLPRCS3_WWE.exe is the name of the adobe 
flash installer (name is an abbreviation for Adobe Flash Producer 
Creative Suite 3 World Wide English) which is a proprietary program that 
costs a few hundred bucks full retail. All commercial adobe products 
(like this one) all require serial numbers for the activation checks, 
even the volume license (AOO) versions, and if it installs without a 
serial number it has *definitely* been modified. Now I don't care if you 
use a cracked program or not but generally cracked programs (especially 
really expensive programs like this one) usually have lots of malware 
hidden in them that can really mess up your system.

> be tucked away in a binary this way.  strings will pull that out so
> you can see it.  eg:
>
> $ strings ADBEFLPRCS3_WWE.exe

Rich Shepard wrote:

>    Good to know. If I go to OfficeDepot, for example, to buy a SD card, what
> should I look for to determine if it's formatted FAT32 and not something
> incompatible?

Doesn't matter what it's formatted as from the store. You can reformat 
them anyway you like. For this, it needs to be Fat32 though. I would buy 
a 1GB card as they will be the cheapest. You're not going to get 
anywhere near as good a price at a shop like that though. You might want 
to call around before you head out, but that's up to you.

-- 
- m0gely

# from Rich Shepard
# on Wednesday 15 October 2008 11:05:

>? ?You've shown me where the problem originates, and I appreciate
> that. Can you now suggest how I can determine where the permissions
> problem is?

Rich,

I don't think I've ever run loadkeys as other than root.  The manpage 
seems to indicate that read permissions on /dev/console are the 
controlling factor (read permissions?!), so `ls -l /dev/console` on 
your various systems is probably going to indicate something different.  
Hopefully, you will find that your old system had your user in a group 
rather than having global read on /dev/console (mine has 0600 perms.)

From lnxknight at gmail.com  Fri Oct 17 19:10:08 2008
From: lnxknight at gmail.com (Matt McKenzie)
Date: Fri, 17 Oct 2008 19:10:08 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] CentOS and windows media format.
In-Reply-To: <20081017171647.96bh0t4w000884s0@web.robinson-west.com>
References: <20081017171647.96bh0t4w000884s0@web.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <da2207740810171910q5e44f981vf8141d534ceb3589@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, Oct 17, 2008 at 5:16 PM, someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:

> Totem could not play
> 'mms://frigg.psu.ds.pdx.edu/files2/fall08/Fant_CS202_101708.wmv'.
>
> No URI handler implemented for "mms".
>
> Anyone know a simple fix for this???  I want to fix totem, the codec
> has to be available from somewhere.
>
>      Michael Robinson
>
>
www.mplayer.hu has a package, ALL codecs for win32.  It is the website for
mplayer, but they have the codecs available separately.
Put them in the proper location (/usr/lib/codecs usually), and Totem may or
may not be able to play it without further fuss.
There may be a specific "plug in" or add on package you need to give Totem
the proper codecs instead,
I know for example in Fedora there are several.
You may need to pull in 3rd party repository for this, such as Livna (this
is true on Fedora, not sure CentOS).

IMHO it is easier and better to use VLC, and mozilla vlc plugin.


-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

Subject: [PLUG] Can't get past my router

I'm sending this from the Comcast web mail interface, using my laptop. At the clinic today I changed my desktop machine from a static IP address to DHCP so I could connect from Free Geek. All was well.

When I got home, I changed back to a static IP address. Now, I can't get past my router. I can ping the router, and I can ping my laptop. I don't think I changed anything else. The gateway address is the correct one for the router. To be more accurate, I didn't change back to a static IP address right away. I didn't think of it at first. However, even with DHCP, I still couldn't get past my router. I've tried ifconfig eth0 down/up with no change.

Any ideas on how to tell the machine it's home again?

--
Regards,

Dick Steffens

Dick,

A quick test would be to turn off your firewall completely in the machine in
question.
If things then work, it means you need to change some firewall settings.
Don't forget to turn it back on.

I concur with the /etc/resolv.conf possibility.
More than likely, when you connected by DHCP, the DHCP server gave your
machine a new resolv.conf file, and when you get home that doesn't work
anymore.  I have run into this, and for laptops that move between networks I
usually keep a backup of my resolv.conf for when I go back home to my static
IP setup.



-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

> More than likely, when you connected by DHCP, the DHCP server gave your
