Sure enough, resolv.conf was changed by dhcp at Free Geek. It has: 

search fglan 
nameserver 192.168.253.11 
nameserver 192.168.253.12 

I have no idea what resolv.conf had before that, and it looks like a backup copy was not made by dhcp. I Linux-Googled [resolv.conf Ubuntu] and found that some other folks have had this problem. I tried copying the concepts they used. Here's what's in resolv.conf now: 

iface eth0 inet static 
address 192.168.0.253 
netmask 255.255.255.0 
network 192.168.0.0 
broadcast 192.168.0.255 
gateway 192.168.0.1 

(Their example was for 192.168.1.x.) 

After saving that, they recommend: 

sudo /etc/init.d/networking restart 

I did that, but it didn't seem to help. 

Does that resolv.conf look right, or is there something missing? 

Now that I know that 

search fglan 
nameserver 192.168.253.11 
nameserver 192.168.253.12 

are from Free Geek, when I look at System>Administration>Network from the Ubuntu menu, I see that I needed to remove them from the DNS Servers and Search Domains sections of Network Settings. I don't know what should go back, though. Would that be something like comcast.net? 

Thanks for all the help.

From plug at the-wes.com  Sun Oct 19 20:01:10 2008
From: plug at the-wes.com (wes)

The search domain is unrelevant in your case. You need to find out what DNS
servers your router uses, and put them into resolve.conf after "nameserver"
on each line.

You should remove the rest of the stuff you put in there, that is not
resolv.conf material.

Here are some comcast DNS servers you can try, if your router doesn't have
other settings:

68.87.69.146
68.87.85.98
68.87.78.130

or, you can try your router's address itself (192.168.0.1), in case it is
acting as a DNS gateway as well.

-wes

your router.  Voila!

From rsteff at comcast.net  Sun Oct 19 21:25:09 2008

Subject: [PLUG] Resolved:  Can't get past my router - resolv.conf fix

References: <869004727.624671224470669176.JavaMail.root@sz0130a.emeryville.ca.mail.comcast.net>	<572487724.625461224471116036.JavaMail.root@sz0130a.emeryville.ca.mail.comcast.net>	<b399d1c10810192001h7c5e9835s59ed0b54c52ca5ad@mail.gmail.com>

Message-ID: <48FC0825.9030904@comcast.net>

Carlos Konstanski wrote:
> Or the easiest way to write a correct resolv.conf file is to let your
> router do it for you.  Set up your laptop to DHCP and get a lease from
> your router.  Voila!
>   
Bingo. Here's what it did:

cat /etc/resolv.conf
search comcast.net
nameserver 192.168.0.1

Then, I switched back to static IP address, and all is well, again.

Thanks for the tip.

Thanks to all for chipping away at this one.


For the benefit of the archives:
When I take the box to the clinic,

1. Copy resolv.conf to resolv.conf.home
2. Change from static IP address to DHCP
3. When I get home, replace the changed resolv.conf with the saved one.
4. Restart networking.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

Sounds like you are referring to OpenWRT(1) and DD-WRT(2), which started off
running on the Linksys WRT54G series routers?
I myself have a Linksys WRT54G v4 (the one that still has Linux internally
as stock, after that they switched to a proprietary OS and less flash RAM
and ROM) running DD-WRT v.24, works like a champ, quite a bit more
configurable than stock, including static DHCP tables among many other
options.
With the newer versions you want to get WRT54GL, which just means it has the
necessary amount of RAM and ROM to run the configurable (and open) Linux OS
on them.  DD-WRT and probably OpenWRT also support many of the newer routers
that are draft-N capable, check the compatibility lists before you buy one
though.
In any case the WRT54GL are not too terribly expensive, and with one of
these more open firmware OS on it you can do almost anything the "big boys"
can do, still in a nice compact and not too power hungry enclosure.

(1) www.openwrt.org
(2) www.dd-wrt.com




-- 
----------
Matt M.
LinuxKnight

From raanders at acm.org  Tue Oct 21 08:52:02 2008
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)
Date: Tue, 21 Oct 2008 08:52:02 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Suggestions for _really_ small business data backup
Message-ID: <48FDFAA2.1030903@acm.org>

I have a customer that for years was able to do their backup by creating 
an CD ISO image (mkisofs) and burn it (cdrecord).  Over the last few 
months it has been failing off and on for no distinctly discernible reason.

The next test is to replace the CD-RW drive but I've tried the process 
with an external CD-RW and still get the same kinds of results.

Tape drives are too expensive and, in my experience, not always reliable 
without _lots_ of TLC.  In fact I moved them from tape backup in a 
desktop system, that had never been tested by actually restoring, to the 
CD-RW and it worked like a charm -- or several years.

I'm considering SD or thumb/jump drives as they will hold all their 
foreseeable data needs or even Bakula over the network to an off site 
system.

Are there other (_very_) inexpensive backup options?  Well besides hope 
and prayer.  :-)


TIA,
Rod
--

drives without losing any data. YMMV. That said, ...

From da6d.fierbaugh at gmail.com  Tue Oct 21 10:00:04 2008
From: da6d.fierbaugh at gmail.com (David Fierbaugh)
Date: Tue, 21 Oct 2008 10:00:04 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Suggestions for _really_ small business data backup
In-Reply-To: <48FDFAA2.1030903@acm.org>
References: <48FDFAA2.1030903@acm.org>
Message-ID: <ce6e472a0810211000ha86b1ebs11607becb3ada16d@mail.gmail.com>

Sounds like they're always doing full backups now. Have you considered
splitting the backups to a periodic (weekly) full backup, and more frequent
incremental backups?

How much data?
How fast does the data change?
Are they backing up pertinent data or shotgun backup of the whole/portion of
the filesystem?

Take a look at what the 'footprint of disaster' they need to be recoverable
from. For example, if the backup media is in the same computer, a single
failure (cup of coffee disaster) can make it go away; in the same room, a
small fire makes it go away; the same building.... ; the same city.... ;
etc.

Does the data need to be encrypted? How do you safely backup decryption
key(s)?

Where are the human bottlenecks? How many people know how to do backup? How
many people know how to restore?

There's always a lot of talk about the right tool(s) to do the backup, and
sometimes the process gets overlooked. If you have great tools in place, but
the process isn't well defined and implemented, then the system doesn't
work.

Rich Shepard (rshepard at appl-ecosys.com) typed this ...
> On Tue, 21 Oct 2008, Roderick A. Anderson wrote:
> 
> > Tape drives are too expensive and, in my experience, not always reliable
> > without _lots_ of TLC.
> 
> Rod,
> 
>    That probably depends on the drives. I've been using Tandburg drives for

And I have a tape drive that has been very reliable for about 5 years
and shows no signs of imminent failure. And it wasn't new when I got it.

But tape drives and media are expensive; you can get a hard drive or USB
flash drive for a LOT less.

> > Are there other (_very_) inexpensive backup options?  Well besides hope
> > and prayer.  :-)
> 
>    I'd go for a separate hard drive (perhaps on a USB connection) with
> Keith's pet 'dervish' (about which I know nothing) or tar.

I'm setting up rsnapshot for a client this week. I like it. It is
similar to dervish in many ways; uses links to save space, cycles
between full and incremental backups in a configurable way, etc. 

A little analysis (as described in an earlier reply) will help you
determine whether or not you need this much complexity. A 2.5" USB hard
drive might be better than rsnapshot, with the existing scripts adapted
accordingly.

From pdxlinux at johnmedway.com  Wed Oct 22 08:21:52 2008
From: pdxlinux at johnmedway.com (pdxlinux at johnmedway.com)
Date: Wed, 22 Oct 2008 08:21:52 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Suggestions for _really_ small business data backup
In-Reply-To: <20081021181348.GN17234@merlot.com>
References: <48FDFAA2.1030903@acm.org>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0810210956320.9645@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<20081021181348.GN17234@merlot.com>
Message-ID: <6.2.1.2.2.20081021221048.0520edf0@mail.johnmedway.com>

Backuppc does similar tricks w/ hard links to conserve space,
and has a pretty luser-friendly GUI for restorations, if that's
of interest. Handles multiple hosts, using samba or rsync or tar,
and also has an archiving function, so you can spit a snapshot
out onto a removable.

Used it for years. On sourceforge. Two thumbs up.

From raanders at acm.org  Wed Oct 22 09:09:45 2008
From: raanders at acm.org (Roderick A. Anderson)
Date: Wed, 22 Oct 2008 09:09:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Suggestions for _really_ small business data backup
In-Reply-To: <48FDFAA2.1030903@acm.org>
References: <48FDFAA2.1030903@acm.org>
Message-ID: <48FF5049.5000502@acm.org>

Roderick A. Anderson wrote:

> I have a customer that for years was able to do their backup by creating 
> an CD ISO image (mkisofs) and burn it (cdrecord).  Over the last few 
> months it has been failing off and on for no distinctly discernible reason.
> 

My thanks to all of you for your very excellent suggestions and 
observations.

I should have gone on-site before posting because it turned out to be a 
case of fat fingers on my part.

This is a small office, just three people, with _some_ computer 
knowledge.  The problem was my fix to another issue  missed a leading 
slash for redirection of STDOUT.  A script was failing not the backup.

I have a simple script they run in the morning "goodmorning" that gives 
a non-threating welcome message, mounts the CD, displays the root level 
directories, then ejects the CD.  They put in a new CD and it is ready 
for the next days back up.

The missing slash seems to have caused the mount to fail ... go figure eh?

The backup is done with a script via a cron.

Again thanks to all for the suggestions and ideas.  I still suspect they 
will have to move on something else as their data grows.  And now I know 
others see the same choices I do.


Rod

> Try mounting /dev/sda directly. There's really no reason to have a partition

Yes that could be.  To get more information about the
partition/filesystem you can try:

  file -s /dev/sda

Install The SleuthKit (apt-get install sleuthkit) and run:

  mmls /dev/sda


HTH,
tim

I don't know what Telenet is/was.
But if you want plain old terminal access, here is one place you can go:
http://sdf.lonestar.org/index.cgi

Note this isn't an ISP, you still need to have your regular ISP access, this
provides free UNIX shell accounts (via telnet).

Besides what kind of info are you trying to access?  If you have an ISP
account who says you have to use a web browser?
Pop open a bash shell and run lynx, mutt, ssh/telnet etc, all work fine.

On Thu, Oct 23, 2008 at 16:25, Matt McKenzie <lnxknight at gmail.com> wrote:
> I don't know what Telenet is/was.

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telenet

i can't tell from that article if it's still around, and i haven't
heard anything about it since the mid-90's.  that doesn't necessarily
mean a thing as to its continued operation though =)

> But if you want plain old terminal access, here is one place you can go:
> http://sdf.lonestar.org/index.cgi


funny thing is, i did dial in to sdf.lonestar.org at 2400bps back in
1992 with a terminal program, which sounds like what Miles is looking
for.  this was before they were even on the internet in realtime (they
had a high-falutin' 21.6kbps UUCP feed for usenet and email).  they're
a stellar operation, and have been free all this time, so definitely
look into them if you need a shell account somewhere.  but i don't
think they do dialup these days...even if they do, they are based in
texas, so it probably won't be a free call.  you'd probably be better
off doing dialup with someone like earthlink who has local access
numbers and then just running a program like putty to access sites
like SDF.

-- 
mute at sdf.lonestar.org for 16 years and counting..

From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Thu Oct 23 17:27:42 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Thu, 23 Oct 2008 17:27:42 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Mounting the G1 Android under Linux,
	and a weird	partition table.
In-Reply-To: <60483.74.203.186.1.1224803553.squirrel@sm.tbruce.com>
References: <c834ec80810231526w675947efl14437df6b6fd3ae3@mail.gmail.com>
	<200810231535.38743.aaron@madebyai.com>
	<20081023224951.GH11669@sentinelchicken.org>
	<60483.74.203.186.1.1224803553.squirrel@sm.tbruce.com>
Message-ID: <20081024002742.GI11669@sentinelchicken.org>

> Wouldn't just
>    # fdisk -l /dev/sda
>
> Tell you everything you want to know?

Well, in many cases yes, but I personally like the output of mmls better
and it supports the following partition types:
	dos (DOS-based partitions [Windows, Linux, etc.])
	mac (MAC partitions)
	bsd (BSD Disklabels [FreeBSD, OpenBSD, NetBSD])
	sun (Sun Volume Table of Contents (Solaris))
	gpt (GUID Partition Table (EFI))

I'm not sure that fdisk does support all of those.

> Or maybe I'm missing something (again?).

Also, the 'file -s ...' command can give you info about the filesystem
that's on the drive if there is no partition table at all.  Both fdisk
and mmls kinda keel over in that case.

tim


From tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org  Thu Oct 23 17:30:58 2008
From: tim-pdxlug at sentinelchicken.org (Tim)
Date: Thu, 23 Oct 2008 17:30:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Mounting the G1 Android under Linux,
	and a weird	partition table.
In-Reply-To: <200810231535.38743.aaron@madebyai.com>
References: <c834ec80810231526w675947efl14437df6b6fd3ae3@mail.gmail.com>
	<200810231535.38743.aaron@madebyai.com>
Message-ID: <20081024003058.GJ11669@sentinelchicken.org>


Oh, also, if the previously suggested inspection tools fail you, you
could try the tools 'gpart' and 'testdisk', both of which are designed
to recover filesystems off of disks when the partition table becomes
corrupt or is overwritten.  This is obviously not the case for you, but
they may be able to identify common filesystems starting in the middle
of the disk even if you can't figure out what partition table is being
used.

Please post back if you figure out how things are laid out... I'm
interested to know myself. =)

tim

On Thu, Oct 23, 2008 at 2:55 PM, Miles Rose <milesros at peoplepc.com> wrote:
> Is there still a Telenet?  I am looking for a phone number where
> I can get plain ol' terminal access, without the baggage of a browser.
> I used to use it for libraries and schools, but seems hard to find now.

  I worked for Telenet back in the 90's.  They got purchased by Sprint
International, and then got transferred to Sprint Data.  The X.25 data
service is probably still around.  It was really popular for overseas
connections.

  I don't know if the terminal PAD service is still being sold.  It
was crazy expensive compared to a dial SLIP connection.  Anyone with
more brains than money converted to SLIP years ago.  You may want to
look for an dial ISP with a shell account.

Steve D...

technically, shell access is only offered to dial-up customers and higher. I
remember when my shell access was revoked as I only had an email account at
the time. I went on vacation a while back and upgraded to dialup for a week.

still thinks I have a dialup account (shhh). I'm guessing something similar
happened to you. But if someone signed up for an email-only today, I'm
pretty sure they wouldn't get shell.

Yes, I understand what you are saying - and I agree because I have seen
OOo's "helpful" code. But in this case OOo is not messing with the URL.
That is, I try a different URL, save the file, upload the new version
to my http://web.pdx.edu/~johj/public_html folder, then reload the page
in Firefox. Firefox correctly sees the changed URL, and if I View
Source in Firefox I can see the <a hrf ...> code correctly reflecting
the URL that I told OOo to make a link to. OOo also allows me to edit
the source, and when I do so I see the URL is correct. That is, it is
not correct, but it is at least what I told OOo to use. In this case it
is a PBKAC error, not a software error.

The most logical URL for this file is

http://web.pdx.edu/~johj/public_html/VOICE004.MP3

But that just gives 404 errors, as do all other variations I have
tried. That is the folder where the index.html file resides, and
browsing with gFTP I can see the VOICE004.MP3 file plain as day.

I have double and triple checked for everything from typos to folder
errors, but every URL I have tried gets a 404 error. 

I e-mailed PSU's help desk hoping they could explain - in case the real
URL is someplace else. Unfortunately, they misunderstood my question
and assumed I had lost the file. 

I'll be on campus this afternoon. Maybe I can go a bit early so I can
sit down at the help desk and show them the problem. That will also
allow me the satisfaction of bitching at them about the 20 MB
attachment limit.

denied at that URL instead of a file not found.  can you change

work in my ftp client, and maybe yours as well.

However, gFTP is not cooperating with my attempt chmod the file.
Right-clicking on it shows an option to chmod it, but when I tell it to

person can do what with the file:

Special	User	Group	Other
----------------------------------------------
Read	Read	Read	Read
Write	Write	Write	Write
Execute	Execute	Execute	Execute

I need to figure out a different way to chmod it.

On further thought, I wonder if I could password protect it. It occurs
to me that the file is a recording of a subject in a dialect study. I
have permission to use his voice data for academic research purposes,
but not to make it available to the entire world. I doubt that it would
be a problem, but it would be nicer to have a little control. I could
just make a password and give it to the students who are entitled to
have the file. But password control probably opens a whole new
dimension to the problem.

At least I think I have the URL right now.

On Fri, 24 Oct 2008 10:31:28 -0700
"chris (fool) mccraw" <gently at gmail.com> dijo:

> On Fri, Oct 24, 2008 at 10:28, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> 
> > But guess what Firefox does now? It opens mplayer and starts to play
> > the file!
> 
> that's what you want!  i mean, it may not be what *you* want, but it
> means the webserver is sending it and is as properly set up as it will
> ever be.  students who want to save the file instead of listening to
> it should be directed to right click and "save as".  if you want it
> not to autoplay, you'll need to zip it or do something else to make it
> not-an-mp3.

Yeah, I thought of zipping it, and I guess that's what I'll have to do.
I just wanted to avoid the complication.

A professor recently asked me to address her class (Phonetics) to
explain to the class how to use the International Phonetic Alphabet on
their computers. I had created ipa4linguists.pbwiki.com last spring,
but the students were having problems with the instructions. The
professor also needed the instruction, so we hooked her laptop up to
the beamer in the classroom and then I proceeded to demonstrate how it
is done. 

Part of the process was to download the Junicode font from
Sourceforge.net. The first problem was that about a quarter of the
class said they clicked the download button on Sourceforge.net, but
couldn't find the file. They didn't know where their browsers were set
to download files to. After downloading it to the professor's computer
we showed them where the file was. Then another quarter of the class
said they had managed to download the file, but it wouldn't install. Of
course, the file is zipped. They had no idea what a zip file is.

You will not go broke overestimating the computer illiteracy of
linguists.

Special thanks to you and Wes for helping me figure it out.

Subject: [PLUG] File upload siteor
In-Reply-To: <20081024100316.1f9c15d2.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20081023182804.924d38b3.johnxj@comcast.net>	<49012EA6.8010000@dslnorthwest.net>	<20081023205905.ee871eb5.johnxj@comcast.net>	<b399d1c10810232139r5cda8f2bw81cb516b636429e6@mail.gmail.com>	<20081023231029.a05eef83.johnxj@comcast.net>	<4901DDD0.3000100@comcast.net>	<20081024092901.9da3e332.johnxj@comcast.net>	<ae2db4620810240936j515d6426u96ce6de3636e7748@mail.gmail.com>
	<20081024100316.1f9c15d2.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <49020D0B.4080006@comcast.net>

John Jason Jordan wrote:
> On further thought, I wonder if I could password protect it. It occurs
> to me that the file is a recording of a subject in a dialect study. I
> have permission to use his voice data for academic research purposes,
> but not to make it available to the entire world. I doubt that it would
> be a problem, but it would be nicer to have a little control. I could
> just make a password and give it to the students who are entitled to
> have the file. But password control probably opens a whole new
> dimension to the problem.
>   
One solution is to create a separate directory on your site and not link 
to it from the main page's index.html file. You give the link to the 
directory to students you want to see it and they can get there. The 
rest of the world won't see it. This isn't good enough for real 
security, but may be good enough for what you are doing. Here's an example:

My personal site is:

http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/

There are only two links on that page. However, I have a few other 
directories on the site, each with their own index.html file. An example is:

http://home.comcast.net/~rsteff/2007-Bathroom-Remodel/

So, simply make a copy of your current index.html file, edit out the 
Language Variation part of the original and save that. Create a new 
directory called LanguageVariation (or something that makes sense to 
you). Move both the mp3 file and the copy of index.html to the new 
directory, making sure the name is changed back to index.html. Edit out 
the IPA4Linguists part, if desired, and you're done.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

I left out some important information in my previous message.

The port has to be configured to 4800 baud with the following format.

Data Format
All modules communicate in standard NRZ asynchronous data for-
mat. This format provides one start bit, seven data bits, one parity bit
and one stop bit for each character.




From wevl at pacifier.com  Fri Oct 24 16:55:35 2008
From: wevl at pacifier.com (Wayne)
Date: Fri, 24 Oct 2008 16:55:35 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] c++ serial port programming
In-Reply-To: <1224887187.5753.7.camel@rich-desktop>
References: <1224887187.5753.7.camel@rich-desktop>
Message-ID: <49026077.3040702@pacifier.com>

rich wrote:
> Are there any c++ programmers that know how to access a device connected
> to the serial port?  Is there any c++ programming material available
> that addresses the com ports?
>
> I have two DGH data acquisition modules that are plugged into ttyS0 aka
> COM1.
>
> The command to read data is "$1RD\r" where $1 is the address of the
> module, RD is the Read Data command and \r is a carriage return.
>   
Rich:
Are you sure you are not trying to utilize an application of some sort?
C programming to control a serial port looks like:

/* Finish UART Initalization */
adr = pci_uart_base + LCR_OFS;
outb( DLAB, adr ); // Set DLAB

adr = pci_uart_base + DLLB_OFS;
outb( BAUD, adr ); // Low byte for baud

adr = pci_uart_base + DLHB_OFS;
outb( 0, adr ); // High byte for baud

adr = pci_uart_base + LCR_OFS;
outb( EIGHT_BITS, adr ); // 8 bits, no parity, 1 stop

adr = pci_uart_base + FCR_OFS; // Clear FIFO buffers
outb( (CLEAR_R_FIFO | CLEAR_T_FIFO ), adr);

adr = pci_uart_base + FCR_OFS; // Enable FIFOs with
outb( ENABLE_FIFO, adr ); // 1 byte trigger on interrupts

adr = pci_uart_base + IER_OFS; // Enable interrupts in UART
outb( 3, adr );

========================================
A pretty good site with useful information on the personal computer 
serial port is:

http://www.beyondlogic.org/serial/serial1.htm

Wayne

client -- satellite -- VPN over DSL -- VPN Server -- corporate network stuff

the tunnel is up, and the site server can see the central corporate LAN, 
the client machines on each of the satellite LANs cannot, and I cannot see 
to them from the corporate LAN or any other satellite LAN. So Something's 
wrong with routing/forwarding, and I just don't have it in my brain to 
figure out. Has anyone done net-to-net openvpn, who could

Current sites are arcane builds of Debian (blech) and we're trying to roll 
out a clean CentOS replacement, to get around my predecessor's iptables and 
other cruft that is I think getting in the way. The central server is 
Smoothwall express, with the "experimental" Zerina build to manage openvpn.

Anyone have hints or time to help/advise?

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Oct 27 12:59:33 2008
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 27 Oct 2008 12:59:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Urgent need for Openvpn help
In-Reply-To: <6.2.1.2.2.20081027100018.068ec8d0@landongxi>
References: <6.2.1.2.2.20081027100018.068ec8d0@landongxi>
Message-ID: <20081027195933.GB6994@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Mon, Oct 27, 2008 at 10:25:34AM -0700, John Medway wrote:

> 
> The problem I'm having, and this is lack of sleep, I'm sure, is that while 
> the tunnel is up, and the site server can see the central corporate LAN, 
> the client machines on each of the satellite LANs cannot, and I cannot see 
> to them from the corporate LAN or any other satellite LAN. So Something's 

"route -n" is your friend.  Every server must have routes to all
the other servers and nets they need to reach, routed through the
appropriate tunX interface.  My central server, connected to 5
other machines, has about 30 routes in it.

Openvpn calls scripts to add the routes.  There client .conf
script should have a line that looks like:

      up ./client_to_server.up

That file ( also in /etc/openvpn) looks like

--------------- client_to_server.up ----------------------
#!/bin/bash
route add -net 172.98.1.0 netmask 255.255.255.0 gw $5
route add -net 172.98.2.0 netmask 255.255.255.0 gw $5
route add -net 172.98.3.0 netmask 255.255.255.0 gw $5
----------------------------------------------------------

There is a corresponding script (with different routes, of course)
on the server.  You will have to fool with the routing to get 
everyone playing together. 

Note, you don't have to get this all exactly right in the scripts
the first time.  In order to experiment, you can start openvpn
without routes,  then issue the same route commands manually
from the command line, as root, and see what goes where.  Remember,
you have to have valid routes for both ends of each path, and from
all ends to all other ends.

You can probably combine some subnets for simplicity;  my network
is small enough (and enough routes are blocked between machines)
that I set up each route separately.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

tables are correct. He concludes:

> ping/http/ssh to/from
>         vpn server (5.2) <-> satellite (222.1) succeeds
>         vpn server (5.2) <-> client (222.254) fails

I'd say that the satellite is the most likely villian. It's either 
blocking or failing to route packets between 192.168.5.x and 
192.168.222.x.

a) Are iptables running on satellite? Running "iptables -L" will tell
    all. Any rules or default policies with a DROP or REJECT target are
    suspect...

b) Do the system logs on satellite give any clue?

c) How's your tcpdump-fu? Running "tcpdump host 192.168.222.254" might
    show you if it's the question or the answer that's not getting
    through...

packets.  You should be more interested in the FORWARD chain.

I can successfully access files on my XP and Win98 laptops from this 
Ubuntu 8.04 machine. I can't go the other way. When I try from XP I get 
a login dialog that asks for user name and password. I give the user 
name for my Ubuntu machine, and the password, but it is not accepted.

When I try from my Win98 laptop, I get a dialog that doesn't as for a 
user name, but instead lists as a "Resource" \\MOONGUIDE\IPC$ . It also 
asks for a password. The response to my normal user password is, "The 
password is incorrect. Try again."

Running "sudo smbpasswd -a" for my normal user and adding the password 
fixed the problem for XP, but not for Win98.

Any ideas on what section of smb.conf I need to tweak for Win98, or any 
other solution?

> When I try from my Win98 laptop, I get a dialog that doesn't as for a

> asks for a password. The response to my normal user password is, "The

> other solution?

> --

>
> Dick Steffens
>

When I would get errors similar to what you're seeing, it was usually
because I hadn't defined anything to share in my smb.conf. Something like
this:

[sharename]
        comment = location, purpose, whatev
        path = /path/to/directory
        read only = no
        browseable = yes
        public = yes
        security mask = 0777
        create mask = 0777
        directory mask = 0777
        inherit acls = Yes


-wes

Here's the similar section from my smb.conf. "*" indicates entries that 
I did not have before reading your e-mail. I added them, but the results 
are still the same. I assume that 0777 and 0775 are close enough to at 
least be able to connect to the machine.


# provide access to /home/rsteff
[rsteff]
    comment = rsteff home directory
    path = /home/rsteff
    read only = no
    browseable = yes
    public = yes
    security mask = 0775            *
    create mask = 0775
    directory mask = 0775
    inherit acls = yes                    *

Regards,

wes wrote:
> Well then. I got nuthin. perhaps post your entire smb.conf?
>   

OK. This is the somewhat modified version of the stock smb.conf that 
comes with Ubuntu 8.04.

To answer a possible question about printers, I don't have any printers 
directly connected to the Ubuntu machine. My printer has its own network 
interface.


#
# Sample configuration file for the Samba suite for Debian GNU/Linux.
#
#
# This is the main Samba configuration file. You should read the
# smb.conf(5) manual page in order to understand the options listed
# here. Samba has a huge number of configurable options most of which
# are not shown in this example
#
# Any line which starts with a ; (semi-colon) or a # (hash)
# is a comment and is ignored. In this example we will use a #
# for commentary and a ; for parts of the config file that you
# may wish to enable
#
# NOTE: Whenever you modify this file you should run the command
# "testparm" to check that you have not made any basic syntactic
# errors.
#

#======================= Global Settings =======================

[global]

## Browsing/Identification ###

# Change this to the workgroup/NT-domain name your Samba server will part of
   workgroup = House

# server string is the equivalent of the NT Description field
   server string = %h server (Samba, Ubuntu)

# Windows Internet Name Serving Support Section:
# WINS Support - Tells the NMBD component of Samba to enable its WINS Server
;   wins support = no

# WINS Server - Tells the NMBD components of Samba to be a WINS Client
# Note: Samba can be either a WINS Server, or a WINS Client, but NOT both
;   wins server = w.x.y.z

# This will prevent nmbd to search for NetBIOS names through DNS.
;   dns proxy = no

# What naming service and in what order should we use to resolve host names
# to IP addresses
;   name resolve order = lmhosts host wins bcast

#### Networking ####

# The specific set of interfaces / networks to bind to
# This can be either the interface name or an IP address/netmask;
# interface names are normally preferred
;   interfaces = 127.0.0.0/8 eth0

# Only bind to the named interfaces and/or networks; you must use the
# 'interfaces' option above to use this.
# It is recommended that you enable this feature if your Samba machine is
# not protected by a firewall or is a firewall itself.  However, this
# option cannot handle dynamic or non-broadcast interfaces correctly.
;   bind interfaces only = true



#### Debugging/Accounting ####

# This tells Samba to use a separate log file for each machine
# that connects
   log file = /var/log/samba/log.%m

# Cap the size of the individual log files (in KiB).
   max log size = 1000

# If you want Samba to only log through syslog then set the following
# parameter to 'yes'.
;   syslog only = no

# We want Samba to log a minimum amount of information to syslog. Everything
# should go to /var/log/samba/log.{smbd,nmbd} instead. If you want to log
# through syslog you should set the following parameter to something higher.
   syslog = 0

# Do something sensible when Samba crashes: mail the admin a backtrace
   panic action = /usr/share/samba/panic-action %d


####### Authentication #######

# "security = user" is always a good idea. This will require a Unix account
# in this server for every user accessing the server. See
# /usr/share/doc/samba-doc/htmldocs/Samba3-HOWTO/ServerType.html
# in the samba-doc package for details.
   security = user

# You may wish to use password encryption.  See the section on
# 'encrypt passwords' in the smb.conf(5) manpage before enabling.
;   encrypt passwords = true

# If you are using encrypted passwords, Samba will need to know what
# password database type you are using. 
;   passdb backend = tdbsam

;   obey pam restrictions = yes

;   guest account = nobody
;   invalid users = root

# This boolean parameter controls whether Samba attempts to sync the Unix
# password with the SMB password when the encrypted SMB password in the
# passdb is changed.
;   unix password sync = yes

# For Unix password sync to work on a Debian GNU/Linux system, the following
# parameters must be set (thanks to Ian Kahan 
<<kahan at informatik.tu-muenchen.de> for
# sending the correct chat script for the passwd program in Debian Sarge).
;   passwd program = /usr/bin/passwd %u
;   passwd chat = *Enter\snew\s*\spassword:* %n\n 
*Retype\snew\s*\spassword:* %n\n *password\supdated\ssuccessfully* .

# This boolean controls whether PAM will be used for password changes
# when requested by an SMB client instead of the program listed in
# 'passwd program'. The default is 'no'.
;   pam password change = yes

# This option controls how nsuccessful authentication attempts are mapped
# to anonymous connections
;map to guest = bad user

########## Domains ###########

# Is this machine able to authenticate users. Both PDC and BDC
# must have this setting enabled. If you are the BDC you must
# change the 'domain master' setting to no
#
;   domain logons = yes
#
# The following setting only takes effect if 'domain logons' is set
# It specifies the location of the user's profile directory
# from the client point of view)
# The following required a [profiles] share to be setup on the
# samba server (see below)
;   logon path = \\%N\profiles\%U
# Another common choice is storing the profile in the user's home directory
;   logon path = \\%N\%U\profile

# The following setting only takes effect if 'domain logons' is set
# It specifies the location of a user's home directory (from the client
# point of view)
;   logon drive = H:
;   logon home = \\%N\%U

# The following setting only takes effect if 'domain logons' is set
# It specifies the script to run during logon. The script must be stored
# in the [netlogon] share
# NOTE: Must be store in 'DOS' file format convention
;   logon script = logon.cmd

# This allows Unix users to be created on the domain controller via the SAMR
# RPC pipe.  The example command creates a user account with a disabled Unix
# password; please adapt to your needs
; add user script = /usr/sbin/adduser --quiet --disabled-password 
--gecos "" %u

########## Printing ##########

# If you want to automatically load your printer list rather
# than setting them up individually then you'll need this
;   load printers = yes

# lpr(ng) printing. You may wish to override the location of the
# printcap file
;   printing = bsd
;   printcap name = /etc/printcap

# CUPS printing.  See also the cupsaddsmb(8) manpage in the
# cupsys-client package.
;   printing = cups
;   printcap name = cups

############ Misc ############

# Using the following line enables you to customise your configuration
# on a per machine basis. The %m gets replaced with the netbios name
# of the machine that is connecting
;   include = /home/samba/etc/smb.conf.%m

# Most people will find that this option gives better performance.
# See smb.conf(5) and 
/usr/share/doc/samba-doc/htmldocs/Samba3-HOWTO/speed.html
# for details
# You may want to add the following on a Linux system:
#         SO_RCVBUF=8192 SO_SNDBUF=8192
   socket options = TCP_NODELAY

# The following parameter is useful only if you have the linpopup package
# installed. The samba maintainer and the linpopup maintainer are
# working to ease installation and configuration of linpopup and samba.
;   message command = /bin/sh -c '/usr/bin/linpopup "%f" "%m" %s; rm %s' &

# Domain Master specifies Samba to be the Domain Master Browser. If this
# machine will be configured as a BDC (a secondary logon server), you
# must set this to 'no'; otherwise, the default behavior is recommended.
;   domain master = auto

# Some defaults for winbind (make sure you're not using the ranges
# for something else.)
;   idmap uid = 10000-20000
;   idmap gid = 10000-20000
;   template shell = /bin/bash

# The following was the default behaviour in sarge,
# but samba upstream reverted the default because it might induce
# performance issues in large organizations.
# See Debian bug #368251 for some of the consequences of *not*
# having this setting and smb.conf(5) for details.
;   winbind enum groups = yes
;   winbind enum users = yes

# Setup usershare options to enable non-root users to share folders
# with the net usershare command.

# Maximum number of usershare. 0 (default) means that usershare is disabled.
;   usershare max shares = 100

# Allow users who've been granted usershare privileges to create
# public shares, not just authenticated ones
;   usershare allow guests = yes

#======================= Share Definitions =======================
# provide access to /
;[public]
;   comment = top level directory of machine
;   path = /
;   read only = no
;   public = yes

# provide access to /home/rsteff
[rsteff]
    comment = rsteff home directory
    path = /home/rsteff
    read only = no
    browseable = yes
    public = yes
    browseable = yes
    security mask = 0775
    create mask = 0775
    directory mask = 0775
    inherit acls = yes

# Un-comment the following (and tweak the other settings below to suit)
# to enable the default home directory shares.  This will share each
# user's home directory as \\server\username
;[homes]
;   comment = Home Directories
;   browseable = yes

# By default, the home directories are exported read-only. Change the
# next parameter to 'no' if you want to be able to write to them.
;   read only = no

# File creation mask is set to 0700 for security reasons. If you want to
# create files with group=rw permissions, set next parameter to 0775.
;   create mask = 0775

# Directory creation mask is set to 0700 for security reasons. If you 
want to
# create dirs. with group=rw permissions, set next parameter to 0775.
;   directory mask = 0775

# By default, \\server\username shares can be connected to by anyone
# with access to the samba server.  Un-comment the following parameter
# to make sure that only "username" can connect to \\server\username
# This might need tweaking when using external authentication schemes
;   valid users = %S

# Un-comment the following and create the netlogon directory for Domain 
Logons
# (you need to configure Samba to act as a domain controller too.)
;[netlogon]
;   comment = Network Logon Service
;   path = /home/samba/netlogon
;   guest ok = yes
;   read only = yes
;   share modes = no

# Un-comment the following and create the profiles directory to store
# users profiles (see the "logon path" option above)
# (you need to configure Samba to act as a domain controller too.)
# The path below should be writable by all users so that their
# profile directory may be created the first time they log on
;[profiles]
;   comment = Users profiles
;   path = /home/samba/profiles
;   guest ok = no
;   browseable = no
;   create mask = 0600
;   directory mask = 0700

;[printers]
;   comment = All Printers
;   browseable = no
;   path = /var/spool/samba
;   printable = yes
;   guest ok = no
;   read only = yes
;   create mask = 0700

# Windows clients look for this share name as a source of downloadable
# printer drivers
;[print$]
;   comment = Printer Drivers
;   path = /var/lib/samba/printers
;   browseable = yes
;   read only = yes
;   guest ok = no

# Uncomment to allow remote administration of Windows print drivers.
# Replace 'ntadmin' with the name of the group your admin users are
# members of.
;   write list = root, @ntadmin

# A sample share for sharing your CD-ROM with others.
;[cdrom]
;   comment = Samba server's CD-ROM
;   read only = yes
;   locking = no
;   path = /cdrom
;   guest ok = yes

# The next two parameters show how to auto-mount a CD-ROM when the
#    cdrom share is accesed. For this to work /etc/fstab must contain
#    an entry like this:
#
#       /dev/scd0   /cdrom  iso9660 defaults,noauto,ro,user   0 0
#
# The CD-ROM gets unmounted automatically after the connection to the
#
# If you don't want to use auto-mounting/unmounting make sure the CD
#    is mounted on /cdrom
#
;   preexec = /bin/mount /cdrom
;   postexec = /bin/umount /cdrom

From creswick at gmail.com  Tue Oct 28 23:17:51 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Tue, 28 Oct 2008 23:17:51 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Odd pathing problem
Message-ID: <c834ec80810282317g333f0d64k91336a6435f60c77@mail.gmail.com>

I just ran into a very annoying pathing/environment problem.  I think
it is best explained with a short example:


 |rogue on escher @ 23:09:50 ~/myapps/android-sdk|
$ which update-alternatives
/usr/sbin/update-alternatives
 |rogue on escher @ 23:12:18 ~/myapps/android-sdk|
$ sudo which update-alternatives
/usr/local/bin/update-alternatives
 |rogue on escher @ 23:12:22 ~/myapps/android-sdk|
$ sudo bash
 |root on escher @ 23:13:34 ~/myapps/android-sdk-linux_x86-1.0_r1|
$ which update-alternatives
/usr/sbin/update-alternatives


The "correct" (sbin) version of update-alternatives is found when I'm
running as me (rogue) or as root, but when I run sudo, the
/usr/local/bin version is found.  What PATH is sudo using? (it's
clearly not my PATH, or root's PATH, is it?)  (I thought I was clever
-- and tried sudo echo $PATH, but that seems to expand $PATH before
invoking sudo, so the information doesn't help.)

This is proving to be extremely annoying, since aptitude makes heavy
use of update-alternatives.

Thanks!
Rogan

All,

I've got a system with a firewire disk attached that's having problems. 
I need to get the kernel to leave the disk alone. The system is 
essentially doing mission-critical routing and the external drive used 
to do backups, but now I just need to free up the CPU and filesystem 
resources it's claiming. I'm getting dozens of messages per second like 
this in the system log:

Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] sd 6:0:0:0: [sdc] Result: 
hostbyte=0x00 driverbyte=0x08
Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] sd 6:0:0:0: [sdc] Sense Key :

Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] end_request: I/O error, dev 
sdc, sector 2026775943
Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] Buffer I/O error on device 
sdc1, logical block 253346985

umount attempts fail with "in use", etc.

TIA for any insight!

-Aaron

> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] sd 6:0:0:0: [sdc] Result:
> hostbyte=0x00 driverbyte=0x08
> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] sd 6:0:0:0: [sdc] Sense Key :
> 0x3 [current]
> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] sd 6:0:0:0: [sdc] ASC=0x4b
> ASCQ=0x0
> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] end_request: I/O error, dev

> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] Buffer I/O error on device
> sdc1, logical block 253346985
>
> umount attempts fail with "in use", etc.
>
> TIA for any insight!
>
> -Aaron
>


The trick I've found is to use umount -l which allows the current operation
to continue, but effectively unmounts the device so that any future
operation won't find it. Then when the current operation finishes, it will
fully release the device and you can safely disconnect it.

-wes

Make sure the username you are using to login to Win98 is the same as
the user you set up in smbpasswd.  You only get one chance to supply
this username when you first login.  More than likely you are never
asked for this username either.  Remember, this is a really lame OS, and
doesn't handle multiple users very well.

> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] sd 6:0:0:0: [sdc] Sense Key :
> 0x3 [current]
> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] sd 6:0:0:0: [sdc] ASC=0x4b ASCQ=0x0
> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] end_request: I/O error, dev
> sdc, sector 2026775943
> Oct 29 07:53:41 tcrouter [821718.370254] Buffer I/O error on device
> sdc1, logical block 253346985
>
> umount attempts fail with "in use", etc.
>
> TIA for any insight!
>
> -Aaron

Perhaps you could blacklist the kernel module (if the support for
firewire is compiled as a module, that is) in /etc/modprobe.conf
and/or modprobe.d/blacklist.  That might require a reboot, though.
You could also try to rmmod the module, but that may fail if you can't
even umount it.

That's all I got.

Carlos

On Wednesday 29 October 2008 08:00:05 am wes wrote:
> The trick I've found is to use umount -l which allows the current operation
> to continue, but effectively unmounts the device so that any future
> operation won't find it. Then when the current operation finishes, it will
> fully release the device and you can safely disconnect it.
>
> -wes

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Oct 29 10:32:35 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 29 Oct 2008 10:32:35 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Banning package(es) for good in Debian and Ubuntu
Message-ID: <c834ec80810291032k5bb01a1cla917ad2781d91050@mail.gmail.com>

I've finally reached the breaking point with GCJ (the Gnu Compiler for
Java).  I have spent more time trying to fix problems related to gcj
than any other linux-related issue, and it keeps. comming. back.  I
would not be at all surprised to find that gcj has actually wasted
more time than has been spent developing it.

I need to find a way to keep gcj (and it's associated libraries,
etc...) from ever being installed again, but that doesn't seem to be
simple.  The only tip I've found are the instructions for holding
packages here: https://help.ubuntu.com/community/PinningHowto but that
doesn't seem to have any effect when I test by trying to install an
application that has a requirement on some gcj library. for example:

$ aptitude install pdftk
<snip>
The following NEW packages will be installed:
  gcj-4.2-base libgcj-common libgcj8-1 libgcj8-1-awt libgcj8-jar pdftk
0 packages upgraded, 6 newly installed, 0 to remove and 135 not upgraded.
Need to get 21.8MB of archives. After unpacking 50.4MB will be used.
Do you want to continue? [Y/n/?] n
Abort.
 |root on gnuwestlake @ 10:12:42 ~|
 $ echo gcj-4.2-base hold | dpkg --set-selections
 |root on gnuwestlake @ 10:13:12 ~|
 $ echo libgcj8-1-awt hold | dpkg --set-selections
 |root on gnuwestlake @ 10:13:22 ~|
 $ echo libgcj8-jar hold | dpkg --set-selections
 |root on gnuwestlake @ 10:13:29 ~|
 $ aptitude install pdftk
<snip>
The following NEW packages will be automatically installed:
  gcj-4.2-base libgcj-common libgcj8-1 libgcj8-1-awt libgcj8-jar
....

I've been using update-alternatives to reset the java version to sun's
java, but that is something that I (literally) have do to on a weekly
basis, since at least some packages seem to reset it for me when they
are installed/upgraded.  There are also many items that need to be
switched with update-alternatives, and I often forget to switch them
all.

Thanks!
Rogan

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Oct 29 11:45:03 2008
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 29 Oct 2008 11:45:03 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] pdftk gcj dependencies (was: Re: Banning package(es) for
	good in Debian and Ubuntu)
Message-ID: <c834ec80810291145o55b38c93ncd72b0fb980a253d@mail.gmail.com>

On Wed, Oct 29, 2008 at 11:30 AM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> On Wed, 29 Oct 2008, Rogan Creswick wrote:
>> $ aptitude install pdftk
>> <snip>
>> The following NEW packages will be installed:
>>  gcj-4.2-base libgcj-common libgcj8-1 libgcj8-1-awt libgcj8-jar pdftk
>
>   While this doesn't answer your ubuntu question, I see that the
> pdftk.Slackbuild file uses gcc on pdftk's Makefile.Generic. No java
> involved. Strange that ubuntu requires it.

The Makefile.Generic delegates to Makefile.Base, which in turn
delegates to java_libs/Makefile, and that invokes a makefile in each
java package, using ${GCJ} (which is defined in Makefile.Generic).

By Java standards, this is an extremely odd way to structure a
project, and it's quite unfortunate that gcj is so tightly coupled
with the build process.  I would like to use pdftk, but I am not about
to put up with gcj for one application.

--Rogan

More confusion.

While I can see some of my Linux machine from my Win98 machines, I can't 
see everything that I expect to see. Plus, directories that should be 
similar get different results.

Example: I have a directory called /home/rsteff/photos that is a mount 
point for one of my partitions. I have another directory called 
/home/rsteff/music that is a mount point for another partition. Both of 
these partitions are mounted at boot time via fstab entries. If I 
navigate to those directories from either the GUI file system browser or 
from a terminal window on the Linux machine I see what I expect to see.

 From my Win98 machines I can see a list of directories shown as music, 
photos, rsteff. If I click on the folder icon next to either music or 
photos I get a password dialog, but if I click on the folder icon next 
to rsteff I change directory to /home/rsteff. Once there, I can navigate 
to photos and look at its contents, but if I navigate to music, it looks 
like the directory is empty.

Checking the properties of both directories I don't see any obvious 
differences, other than permissions, but either should be sufficient to 
show the contents. (I did try chmod 777 music, but it didn't affect 
being able to see the directory from Win98.)

rsteff at moonguide:~$ ls -l
<...>
drwxr-xr-x  4 rsteff rsteff    4096 2008-06-06 11:46 music
<...>
drwxrwxrwx  7 rsteff rsteff    4096 2008-10-01 15:13 photos
<...>

Any other thoughts?

From wamorita at hevanet.com  Wed Oct 29 17:52:06 2008

References: <490754BC.10508@comcast.net>	<b399d1c10810281242r606afee0w143335a59420f480@mail.gmail.com>	<490781B8.2020708@comcast.net>	<b399d1c10810281437t48ba6ca7h2ca779f4e3bcbfd0@mail.gmail.com>	<490787EE.9020209@comcast.net>	<b399d1c10810281507r68a53081h62f860a30d60ae0@mail.gmail.com>	<49079A4F.9060600@comcast.net><1225292594.3530.42.camel@localhost.localdomain>	<490880E3.7070707@comcast.net><821BAC7EDFA8430FB9A6EED4B7321776@RAMA>
	<4909037F.7070904@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <3680322121904B2D9618DB5D4A2A3264@RAMA>

Richard

The files in the Samba share need group/ownership of nobody:nobody.
Try logging in as root on your Samba machine (e.g. sudo bash)
"cd" to the mount point for the share
Execute "chown nobody:nobody -R *"

- Bill Morita 

wamorita at hevanet.com 
Home: (503) 697-6994
Cell: (503) 260-3876

My thought before doing that was that it would be a major change to a 
lot of files and I ought to test it somehow. So, I copied one album's 
worth of files to another directory, /home/rsteff/Music. Then, before 
changing ownership on it, I looked for it from my Win98 machine. It was 
there, so that test would not have proven anything. Just for yuks, I 
also looked at /home/rsteff/music from Win98 and found the directory I 
had just made a copy of! I could go through and make copies of all the 
music and put them in Music, but there isn't enough room.

Also for yuks, I deleted the copy and then did a refresh on Win98. The 
copy in music disappeared, too. That's when I remembered the case 
insensitivity of Windows. I changed the name of Music to notMusic, and 
now the files in music are seen from Win98.

Thanks for triggering this re-discovery.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens
 



From rsteff at comcast.net  Wed Oct 29 18:37:21 2008
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Wed, 29 Oct 2008 18:37:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Resolved: Samba shared directory looks empty from Win98
Message-ID: <49090FD1.8090100@comcast.net>

A clearer explanation of the problem and the solution:

When viewing my Linux machine from my Win98 machine I could see the 
directory /home/rsteff/music, but it appeared to be empty. I created the 
directory music as a mount point to a partition full of mp3 files. I 
might have chosen the name Music, but there was already a directory by 
that name, created during the installation of Ubuntu 8.04.

Windows filenames are case insensitive. My guess is that when WinXX 
finds two files of the same name it will see the first one, and ignore 
the second. Since Music was created before music, I assume that's why it 
saw the empty directory. I suppose that I should have just made Music 
the mount point, but at the time I was still new to Ubuntu and didn't 
know if some program would try to use Music later on.

Lesson: remember that case matters to Linux but not to Windows and plan 
accordingly if the Linux machine will be part of a network that includes 
Windows machines.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Oct 31 09:49:38 2008

On Fri, 31 Oct 2008 08:05:43 -0700
"Richard C. Steffens" <rsteff at comcast.net> dijo:

> John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > True to its word, Canonical released Ubuntu Intrepid Ibex 8.10 today,
> > October 30.
> >   
> Having just installed Hardy Heron, my first install of any Ubuntu, 
> what's the recommended philosophy regarding upgrades? Should I switch to 
> II right away, or is it better to let it mellow for a month or more?
> 
> How good is Ubuntu about not messing with stuff I would expect to be 
> left alone? I.e., will I have to re-install VMware, and programs I've 
> installed from source? Will it figure out which ones I've installed from 
> synaptic and from Applications>Add/Remove?

I've been using Ubuntu steadily for several years and usually upgrade
within a few days of the release of a new version. But before I do the
upgrade I download and burn the live CD for it and give it a try.
Sometimes I'll wait as much as a month if I am in the middle of
something at school where the computer is critical to me.

Every time I have upgraded I have found that all my installed
applications ran just fine afterwards, with only an occasional
exception. For example, after upgrading from Dapper to Gutsy I
discovered that drag and drop of text no longer worked in OpenOffice.
That was because the upgrade to Gutsy also upgraded OOo. After
considerable research I discovered that the problem was limited to only
a handful of x86_64 users. Bug reports were filed, but there were so
few of us affected by it that no fix was available until I upgraded to
Hardy. None of us ever figured out exactly how Ubuntu screwed it up,
but we did determine that it affected all versions of OOo - even going
back to the previous version or installing a new version of OOo did not
help. And it affected only Ubuntu x86_64 users; there were no reports
of the problem when using any other distro or 32-bit users. The strange
part is that I had reports from dozens of Ubuntu x86_64 users who were
not affected by the problem. 

The point I am laboriously trying to make is that the dist-upgrade will
also upgrade the applications that come with Ubuntu. Sometimes the new
version of one of these applications will have issues. However, that is
rare. More likely the new version will have new features, work better,
or solve problems that the old version had.

The dist-upgrade will not upgrade any applications that are not
automatically installed by Ubuntu. I have lots of programs that I have
installed and I have never had a problem with any of them after a
dist-upgrade.

As for the operating system itself, I have never had a new version fail
to work at least as well as the old one with my hardware. And usually
the dist-upgrade fixes something that has been a problem. For example,
my bluetooth mouse dies every couple of days and I have to reconnect it
from the terminal. But I just tried the Intrepid CD and I note that the
GUI for connecting bluetooth devices is completely new, plus it took me
only a moment to connect the mouse while running the live CD. I am
hoping that the dead mouse problem has finally been fixed too. 

I do not use VMware, but I do use Vitualbox. I have found that whenever
I upgrade a kernel - even just an update - I cannot run any of my guest
OSs until I go into Synaptic and install new headers for Virtualbox. 

And one more story is worth relating. Once about a year ago I was doing
an apt-get dist upgrade and I lost my network connection in the middle
of it (thanks a lot, Comcast). The result was a godawful mess that took
days of help from people on this list to repair. I learned a lot about
repairing broken systems, but life would been nicer if it hadn't been
necessary. So don't try it if you are in a coffee shop on their
sometimes flaky wireless connection. Also, it takes quite a while for
all the stuff to download, so speed of the connection is also important.

I plan to upgrade my laptop to Intrepid this weekend. Meantime I am
watching the Ubuntu forums. If there are any problems they will show up
there for sure.

Ubuntu upgrades have *always* broken something for me.  Usually audio
fails to work, but once in a while the printing system will just stop
sending data to devices, or some other thing will go wrong.

Power management has never worked properly after an upgrade, but that
has been tied to the UI layer since I've been using Ubuntu (3-4 years
now) and I don't like the default UI options, so that has always been
an issue of sorts.

The issues are not much worse than any other distro (Debian's stabler
two editions are better, IMO).  Overall, I've been disappointed with
some of the decisions the Ubuntu folks have made inorder to meet their
self-imposed release schedule.  I'd much rather get it when it's
ready, rather than getting whatever they have at 6-month intervals.

From plug at the-wes.com  Fri Oct 31 11:04:45 2008
From: plug at the-wes.com (wes)
Date: Fri, 31 Oct 2008 11:04:45 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Wireshark help
In-Reply-To: <4a0b36aa0810311057u1544b995k363898cd5d39a756@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, Oct 31, 2008 at 10:57 AM, VY <vyau5678 at gmail.com> wrote:

> A few years ago, i was able to do that with ettercap when Wireshark was

>

If your network uses a switch to connect computers together, you won't be
able to see any traffic to/from any boxes other than the one you are running
wireshark on. A switch only sends traffic to its destination, not to
anywhere else. You will need to either use a hub to connect to the machines
you want to monitor, or configure your switch to set up a monitoring port,
if it supports that.

-wes


From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Oct 31 11:07:59 2008
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 31 Oct 2008 11:07:59 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Wireshark help
In-Reply-To: <4a0b36aa0810311057u1544b995k363898cd5d39a756@mail.gmail.com>
References: <4a0b36aa0810311057u1544b995k363898cd5d39a756@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <alpine.OSX.2.00.0810311101340.17421@guild.galois.com>

On Fri, 31 Oct 2008, VY wrote:

> I have launched wireshark on my Linux box and trying to capture and 
> analyze network traffic. What I want is not to analyze the Linux box 
> to/from network traffic but analyze other box(es) on the network and 
> their traffic to/from the outside world as well as to/from the linux 
> box.
>
> So far, all I could capture is to/from the Linux host to/from any 
> boxes on my network but fail to capture any traffic out of other 
> boxes to other hosts.

You'll want to read about the differences between switches and hubs.

Hubs distribute all of the data they receive to all the network 
devices to which they are connected:

   http://www.networkclue.com/hardware/network/switches-vs-hubs.aspx

Switches on the other hand, need to be specifically configured with 
port mirroring for a single machine to see all traffic. Different 
networking vendors use differnt terminology for this configuration 
(SPAN port, Roving Analysis Port, ...).

Chances are, your Wireshark machine is connected to the network using 
a switch...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> http://www.madboa.com/


From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Fri Oct 31 11:14:36 2008
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Fri, 31 Oct 2008 12:14:36 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Wireshark help
In-Reply-To: <4a0b36aa0810311057u1544b995k363898cd5d39a756@mail.gmail.com>
References: <4a0b36aa0810311057u1544b995k363898cd5d39a756@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0810311204150.6740@sphinktop.pippiandcarlos.com>

On Fri, 31 Oct 2008, VY wrote:

> Date: Fri, 31 Oct 2008 10:57:23 -0700
> From: VY <vyau5678 at gmail.com>
> Reply-To: "General Linux/UNIX discussion and help;	civil and on-topic"
>     <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> To: plug at lists.pdxlinux.org
> Subject: [PLUG] Wireshark help
> 
> Hi:
>
> I have launched wireshark on my Linux box and trying to capture and analyze
> network traffic.
> What I want is not to analyze the Linux box to/from network traffic but
> analyze other box(es) on the network and
> their traffic to/from the outside world as well as to/from the linux box.
>
> So far, all I could capture is to/from the Linux host to/from any boxes on
> my network but fail
> to capture any traffic out of other boxes to other hosts.
>
> Does anyone know the right filter rules for doing that?
> A few years ago, i was able to do that with ettercap when Wireshark was
> still known as Ethereal
> but things have been re-arranged and I don't see to find the docs for
> describing that.
>
> Thanks
>
> --Vincent

To capture the traffic that you are after, you must use appropriate
network gear.  If you want to see the packets that are meant for a
different computer, you and that computer must be plugged into an
old-style hub together.  Modern switches have enough brains to route
packets by MAC address to only the port for which the packet is meant.
An old-style hub has none of these brains, therefore all packets hit
all ports.  That's why a lot of network geeks keep an old 10 megabit
hub in their gear bag.

The other option is to do your packet tracing on the gateway machine.
Naturally it sees packets from every computer on the network, for all
packets must go through it.  There is an exception to this: two
computers plugged into the same switch can communicate with each other
directly through the switch, without the packets going through the
gateway.  This is a side-effect of switches having enough brains to
route packets by MAC address, as long as the MAC address is in the
switch's MAC table.  But if you are interested in packets between LAN
machines and the internet, the gateway will see all of them.  But it
is highly discouraged to run wireshark on a machine that has a direct
route to the outside world.  Instead, run tcpdump and open the
resulting dump file in wireshark on another machine.

Carlos


From gently at gmail.com  Fri Oct 31 11:26:27 2008
From: gently at gmail.com (chris (fool) mccraw)

On Fri, Oct 31, 2008 at 10:57, VY <vyau5678 at gmail.com> wrote:

> So far, all I could capture is to/from the Linux host to/from any boxes on
> my network but fail
> to capture any traffic out of other boxes to other hosts.

While all the answers so far have touched upon the basic truth
(sniffing on a hub is trivial), sniffing on a switch is not
impossible.  there are a couple of ways of doing so, one optimal (port
mirroring) and unlikely to be possible on cheap networking hardware,
and one which is technically exploitative behavior and prone to
network interruptions (arp cache poisoning).  nonetheless i have
personally had both working with enough success (from a linux machine)
to debug an issue.  a better explanation and comparison than i'd be
able to make off the cuff is here:

http://www.chrissanders.org/?p=121

ettercap and dsniff are the linux tools i've used in the past.

caveat emptor:  using arp cache poisoning may break your network for
non-trivial periods and will certainly piss off your network
administrator.  but hopefully you're your network administrator and
know how to clean up after yourself.  NB this may involve more than
just rebooting the switch--network clients are lied to and the lie may
take awhile to time out depending on OS/settings.

Vincent,

If you are plugged into a switch, you are wasting your time.
(Unless your switch is one with either a special port or specially
configurable)
If you have your network on a hub, you will be able to see the LAN traffic.

	<3680322121904B2D9618DB5D4A2A3264@RAMA>
Message-ID: <490B83AD.4070507@comcast.net>

A very helpful resource for creating smb.conf is to be found at:

http://us1.samba.org/samba/docs/man/Samba-Guide/

The first example showed me what I was missing in my smb.conf:

guest ok = Yes.

Other confusion came about from the proliferation of standards. Somebody 
once said, "The great thing about standards is that there are so many to 
choose from." As with Windows, in Nautilus there is a way to mark 
directories so that they may be "shared" by other computers. When that 
is done, they will appear in the tree in Windows Explorer. I had marked 
a few directories that way before my attempts to reconfigure Samba. 
Those entries could be clicked on, but always asked for a password. On 
XP, I could specify the user name, and it accepted my password. On 
Win98, I could not change the user name from IPC$, and not knowing 
IPC$'s password, those attempts to connect always failed. Using

gksu nautilus

I have un-marked the "shared" directories, and they no longer appear in 
Windows Explorer. I had never marked my home directory that way, thus it 
was available from those Windows machines on which the user name is also 
rsteff. Now, it's also available from my wife's machine. I realize that 
it would also be available to the entire Internet if it weren't for my 
router, or to any machine I allow to plug in to my home network. That's 
an acceptable level of security, here.

My needs are simple: I want to be able to get to any files in my Linux 
home directory from any other machine in the house. I don't need to 
share any printers, since my printer is a stand-alone network device. 
The following is my very simple smb.conf:

rsteff at moonguide:~$ cat /etc/samba/smb.conf
#
# Configuration file for the Samba suite based on the Samba-Guide, Samba 
by example
# found at:
# http://us1.samba.org/samba/docs/man/Samba-Guide/
#

#======================= Global Settings =======================

[global]
  workgroup = House
  security = SHARE
[rsteff]
  path = /home/rsteff
  read only = No
  guest ok = Yes
rsteff at moonguide:~$

Thanks to all who offered ideas. Eventually, they got me to search for a 
HOWTO style of setting up smb.conf.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

I finally got around to giving this a try. I followed the instructions, 
replacing "edgy" with "hardy" in the appropriate places. In addition to 
IE6,I was given the option of also loading IE7.

IE7 doesn't run well. It starts, but never connects to any web site.

IE6 runs. I was able to log in to the RMLS realtors' web site, check out 
some listings, and log out. There were only two issues: 1) loading of 
images was a bit choppy, and definitely slower than on my XP laptop, and 
2) IE6 did not completely shut down. I had to find kill it from a 
separate terminal window.

While it's not ready for everyday use, it is a step closer to being able 
to work in real estate using Linux.

Thanks for the pointer.

I used to run IE6 on Ubuntu x86_64 with Crossover Office. It ran just
fine. It took a long time to launch it, but otherwise there were no
problems at all. And the launch time was like twice as long as it takes
to launch Firefox, due undoubtedly to the fact that it had to start
WINE/Crossover first.

Having said that, as I recall I ran it in a Win98 bottle, not a 2000 or
XP bottle. I dimly recall that it didn't work as well in other kinds of
bottles.

Crossover Office is not free, but Codeweavers contributes back to the
WINE project. I did this a couple years ago, and the reason was because
at the time getting WINE to run in 64-bit Ubuntu was problematic.
Crossover, however, installed without a hitch.
